Safety measures in using electricity

Electricity plays a vital role in our life. It is one of the most important and
convenient source of energy at homes and industries. However, we daily come
across reports regarding hazards of electricity. Use of electricity can prove to be
very dangerous if certain precautions and safety measures are not observed in the
design of electrical devices and in handling out. The precautions that must be
considered while using electricity are mentioned below.

i) Use wires of high quality, proper amperage and having good insulating

material.

ii) Cover all naked wires and joints with insulating tape.
iii) All connections at plugs, switches, sockets must be tight

iv) Replace the defective plugs, switches and sockets.

Science- 10 82


v) Never touch any part of the circuit without putting on rubber shoes or rubber
gloves.

vi) Use fuse of proper rating and material.

vii) All electrical appliances must be properly earthed

viii) Connect switches and fuse to live wires.

ix) Put off the main switch in case of short circuiting and fire

x) Do not use water as fire extinguishers in case of fire due to electricity.

Electric power consumption

The knowledge regarding the calculation of power consumption in domestic
electrification is also equally important. The bill of power consumption in our
house should be paid regularly to the electricity authority in time. The electricity
consumed in house is known as kilowatt hour meter. Kilowatt hour is the unit of
power consumption. For the sake of simplicity, it is customarily called unit only.
In fact the power consumed per hour by an electric appliance of power one
kilowatt is called one kilowatt hour.

1 unit power consumption = | kilowatt hour
= 1 kw x Ihr = 1000watt x 60 x 60 se. = 3.6 x 10°J

=PXNxt

required to know how many of the electric appliances (Electric bulb, heater, fan,

etc.) are operating, their total power and time in hours per day the appliances are

used. Since each appliance is operated in different periods of time, it is better to

calculate the unit consumed by these appliances separately. Let us study the
following example.

Science- 10 88


unit7 Classification of Elements

Introduction

In the ancient times, there were very few elements so their individual study was possible
but with the development of science, more and more elements were discovered. We have
almost 118 elements till today so the task of studying them one by one has become very
difficult and time consuming. Therefore, the necessity of classifying them on the basis of
their similarities and dissimilarities were felt by the scientific community. The table so
formed by the arrangement of elements on the basis of their r and dissimilar
properties is called periodic table.

 

  

Several attempts were made by many scientists to classify the elements and make their
study systematic. Some of the scientists were Antoine Lavoisier, John Dalton, Johann
Dobereiner, John Newlands and Lothar Meyer. All of them have contributed to bring the
classification of elements in present condition. Among them, Dmitri Mendeleev, a
Russian scientist had done really comprehensive and illustrative work in the construction
of periodic table but with the passage of time, some demerits were found. So, Henry
Mosely came up with modem periodic table that has removed almost all the demerits of
Mendeleev’s periodic table.

Periodic table is one of the greatest discoveries of chemistry that made it possible to study
all the elements systematically.

Mendeleev’s periodic table

Dmitri Mendeleev studied the physical and chemical properties of all 63 elements known
at that time. He found out that the physical and chemical properties of the elements
change with increasing atomic weight but similar properties repeat at regular interval. On
the basis of this finding, he stated his periodic law that was termed as Mendeleev’s
periodic law. It states that the physical and chemical properties of elements are
periodic function of their atomic weights.

On the basis of this law, he arranged 63 clements of that time in the order of their
increasing atomic weights. He also kept in mind that the similar elements fall one below
another in the same group. In this way, similar elements falling in the same vertical
column are called group and dissimilar elements falling in the same horizontal rows are
called periods. The table so formed is called Mendeleev’s periodic table. There were eight
groups and seven periods in Mendeleev’ periodic table

While arranging the elements in periodic table some of the slots remain empty because
the elements that match the properties of those particular slot are yet to be discovered.

So to maintain the symmetry of the table he kept certain gaps and predicted their physical
and chemical properties on the basis of periodic law. The greatest achievement of
Mendeleev’s periodic law was that three of the elements predicted by Mendeleev were

Science- 10 89


discovered in his lifetime. These elements are scandium, gallium and germanium that
were named as Eka boron, Eka alumunium and Eka silicon respectively by Mendeleev.
The properties of these elements also match as those predicted by Mendeleev.

Table 1: a portion of Mendeleev’s Periodic Table

A portion of Mendeleev's Periodic Table
i ut Iv Vv vi vu

 

* Named by Mendeev 1. Eka - Aluminium
2. Eka - Boron
3. Eka - Silicon
4. Eka - Manganese

Merits of Mendeleey’s Periodic Table
1. Systematic study of elements

Mendeleev did the most comprehensive and illustrative job in the field of
classification of elements at that time, which helped in the scientific and systematic
study of all known 63 elements.

 

2. Gaps in the periodic table

While arranging the elements on the basis of increasing atomic weight, Mendeleev
had left some gaps in his periodic table because the elements whose properties match
with that particular slot were not discovered then. He predicted that those elements
would be discovered in future. He also determined their physical and chemical
properties. Three of those predicted elements have been discovered during his
lifetime. Those elements were scandium, gallium and germanium that were named as
Eka boron, Eka aluminium and Eka silicon respectively by Mendeleev.

Science- 10 90


3. Correction of wrong atomic weight

Mendeleev found atomic weight of some of the elements were doubtful during the
construction of the periodic table. Atomic weights of some of the elements didn’t
justify their position so he studied their atomic weight once again and found they
were wrong and had given the correct atomic weight. For example, the atomic weight
of beryllium (Be), caesium (Cs), uranium (U), gold (Au) and platinum (Pt) have been
corrected by Mendeleev.

Demerits of Mendeleev’s Periodic Table

1. Position of hydrogen

Hydrogen has properties such as having only one electron in its outermost shell and
tendency of donating one electron in order to form a positively charged ion. These
properties match to that of group T' while the properties such as existing in gaseous
state and tendency of gaining one electron in order to become stable by gaining
duplet state which resembles with that of group VII but Mendeleev placed hydrogen
in group I without any explanation. Thus the position of hydrogen is controversial in
Mendeleev’s periodic table.

2. Position of isotopes

Since Mendeleev has arranged the elements in the order of increasing atomic weights,
the isotopes of the same element should be kept in different groups since they have
different atomic weight. Isotopes are the atoms of the same clement with different
atomic weights due to difference in number of neutron present in their nucleus. For
example, hydrogen has three isotopes protium (atomic weight: 1), deuterium (atomic
weight: 2) and tritium (atomic weight: 3).

3. Anomalous pair

Though Mendeleev’s Periodic table is formed on the basis of increasing atomic
weight of elements, there are some instances where the elements with more atomic
weights are kept after the element with less atomic weight. For example, Co (58.9) is
placed before Ni (58.7) and Te (127.6) is placed before I (127.0).

4. Separation of similar elements and grouping of dissimilar elements

In Mendeleev’s periodic table, similar elements such as Au and Pt, Cu and Hg, Ag
and Ti are separated or are placed in different groups while the elements such as Cu,
Ag and Au with dissimilar properties are placed along with alkali metals (Li, Na, K)
in the group 'I'.

Modern Periodic Table

In 1913, Henry Mosely and his team studied the physical and chemical properties of
elements and found out the atomic number of an element determine the property of an
clement rather than its atomic weight. Then, he arranged the elements on the basis of
increasing atomic number and most of the anomalies of Mendeleev’s periodic table were
removed automatically. He proposed modern periodic law that states — “The physical

Science- 10 91


and chemical properties of the elements are the periodic function of their atomic
number.”

This way in modern periodic table, the elements having similar properties fall one after
another in the same vertical column called group. While elements having gradual change
in properties are kept one after another in the horizontal rows called periods. This way a
table was constructed by arranging the elements in the order of their increasing atomic
number, which is known as modern periodic table.

Science- 10 92


Modern periodic table has been very helpful in the correction of demerits of Mendeleev’s
periodic table, The defects of Mendeleev’s periodic table were corrected as given belo

 

1. Position of hydrogen
In modem periodic table also, hydrogen is placed in group I though it has properties
of group VII, but explanation was given that more properties of hydrogen match with
that of group I. However, the hydrogen placement is still controversial and not
explained satisfactorily.

2. Position of isotopes

Since the elements of modem periodic table are arranged on the basis of increasing
atomic number, isotopes of one element can be placed in one place as they have same
atomic number and different atomic weights.

3. Position of lanthanides and actinides

The lanthanides and actinides are placed at the bottom of the table in a separate block
(£) in order not to increase the width of the table unnecessarily.

4, Position of metals
Different types of alkali metals, alkaline earth metals, transition metals and coinage
metals are separately placed in different group so that their study becomes more
systematic and proper.

5. Correction in the wrong position of elements

The position of Cobalt before Nickel and Tellurium before Iodine are also corrected
automatically by arranging the elements in the order of increasing atomic number.

Position of elements in the periodic table

1. Position of hydrogen

Hydrogen has dual properties. It resembles both with groups IA and VIIA. It has one
electron in its outermost shell so it can donate one electron to form a +ve ion just like
that of alkali metals so it can be placed in the group IA while it exists in gaseous state
and can gain one electron in stable state so it can be placed in the group VITA, but in
modern periodic table hydrogen is placed in group IA because more of its characters
match with that of group IA elements.

2. Position of metals, non metals and metalloids

Metals are elements that donate electron to form positive ion and conduct heat and
electricity. Non- metals are the elements which gain electron and do not conduct heat
and electricity. Metalloids are elements, which have the characters of both metals and
non- metals, They are placed in between metals and non-metals,

In modern periodic table the position of metals, non-metals and metalloids is very
clear. As we move from left to right in a period, the metallic character decreases

Science- 10 93


while non metallic character increases. Therefore, the elements of group IA, IIA and
IIIA are mostly metals. IVA are metalloids and VA, VIA and VIIA are non-metals.

Group IA contains most reactive metals known as alkali metals such as Li, Na, K etc.
They form alkali when dissolved in water, group IIA contains alkaline earth metals,
for example, Be, Mg, Ca etc. They also form alkali when dissolved in water and
found in rocks of the earth. Group VIIA contains most reactive non-metals known as
halogens.

3. Position of lanthanides and actinides

The 14 elements from cerium (58) to lutetium (71) after lanthanum (57) are called
lanthanides. They are rare earth elements. The other 14 elements from thorium (90) to
Lawrencium (103) after actinium (89) are called actinides. They are radioactive
metals. They are placed in the f-block in the separate table below the main body of
periodic table due to their different properties. It is modified to avoid unnecessary
sidewise expansion of the periodic table.

4. Position of transition elements

A set of metallic elements occupying a central block (group IB to VII B and VII) in
the periodic table is called transition elements. They show variable valency and a
strong tendency to form co-ordinate compound and many of their compounds are
coloured. They are also called d-block elements since their valence electrons are
filled in d orbitals.

your classroom.

Groups and Periods in periodic table
Groups

The vertical columns of the periodic tables are called groups in modern
periodic table. There are total of 18 groups. Elements having similar
properties are placed in a group. Each of the group from I to VII are divided
into A and B subgroups. There are three columns of group VIII. There is
one zero group which includes inert or noble gases having valency zero.

Science- 10 94


Characteristics of groups

 

1. The electronic configuration of the elements in a group is similar and the

valance shell contains same number of electrons and consequently they | 14

 

have same valency and similar properties. li

 

2. The atomic size of elements increase down the group hence nuclear
attraction force decreases from top to bottom in a group. This causes | Na

 

loosing of electron easily due to weaker attraction force between nucleus
and valence electrons. Therefore, the electro positivity (tendency of | K

 

 

 

donating electrons) or metallic characters of the elements increases in a
group. While the electronegativity (tendency of gaining electrons) decreases down
the group since the nuclear attraction power decreases down the group, more
electrons cannot be attracted towards the valence shell.

Elements belonging to the group IA are called alkali metals, They have only one electron
in their outermost shell so they show their reactivity by losing one electron or they are
electropositive in nature. As we move down the group the atomic size increases and
nuclear attraction force decreases, the valence electrons are loosely held so donating
electrons becomes easier as we move down the group. So reactivity or the electro
positivity increases down the group.

 

 

K>Na>Li

Elements belonging to group VIIA are called halogens. They are the | VIA
most reactive non-metals and show their reactivity by gaining one
electron. They are electronegative and give electronegative ions | F
(anion), a

As we move down the group, atomic size of the elements increases,
nuclear attraction power decreases so electrons can be attracted with | Br
lesser energy as we move down the group. Hence, reactivity or
electronegativity of group VIIA elements decreases down the group | I
according to the order.

F>Cl>Br>1

Periods

The horizontal rows of the periodic table are called periods. In modern periodic table,
there are seven periods. Elements having gradual change in properties are placed in a
iod. On the basis of number of elements present, periods of modem periodic table are
ded into four types.

 

1. Very short period - 1" period. It contains 2 elements.
Short periods - 2" and 3“ periods. They contain 8 elements.

Long periods - 4" and 5" periods. They contain 18 elements.

RYN

Very long periods - 6" and 7 periods. They contain 32 and 26 elements
respectively.

Characteristics of periods

1. The gradual change in electronic configuration of element in a period is observed.
The number of shell remains same but the number of valence electrons increases as
we move from left to right in a period. Therefore, the valency also changes as 1, 2, 3,
4, 3,2, 1, 0.as we move from left to right in a period.

2. The atomic size or radii of the elements decreases as we move from left to right in a
period since the increase in number of electrons adds to the same shell. With the
increase in atomic number, number of proton and neutron in the nucleus also increase
thereby increase in the electrostatic force of attraction. So the nuclear attraction
power increases from left to right in a period as the result, shells are held more tightly
towards nucleus hence atomic size decreases from left to right in a period. Due to
which, electropositivity (tendency of donating electron) decreases while
electronegativity (tendency of gaining electrons) increases. Or metallic characteristic
decreases and non-metallic characteristic increases as we move from left to right in a
period. A part of periodic table, period 3, is given below.

 

 

Groups TA TIA MA |IVA | VA VIA | VITA | Zero

 

Elements Na Mg Al Si P Ss cl Ar

 

Electronic

we | 281 | 2.8.2 [283 | 284 |285 | 286 | 28.7 | 2.8.8
configuration

 

Valency 1 2 3 4 3 2 1 0

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

In the table above, 3" period of modern periodic table is shown. As we can see, atomic
number of the elements increases from left to right in a period. The number of electrons
also increases although there is same number of shells. Thus, the number of protons also
increase which in turn increases nuclear attraction force. As the result, nuclear attraction
force increases and atomic size decreases. Hence, electro positivity or metallic characters

Science- 10 96


decreases from Na to Al i.e. group IA to IIIA. Si shows metalloids characters, i.e. group
IVA and non-metallic characters increases from P to Cl, ie. group VA to VIIA and Ar
shows inert behavior, i.e. group zero.

Blocks in periodic table

The elements in periodic table are placed in four different blocks on the basis of last
electron entering into valence subshell.

a,

s-block elements

When the last electron of an element enters into s-subshell, it is known as s-block
elements. The elements belonging to group IA and IIA are s-block elements.
They have 1 or 2 electrons in their outermost subshell so they are metallic
electropositive in nature.

p- block elements

Science- 10 98


When the last electron of an element enters into p-subshell, it is known as p-
block element. The elements belonging to IIIA, IVA, VA, VIA, VIIA, and zero
group are p-block elements. They have maximum 6 electrons in their outermost
subshell. They may be electropositive or electronegative. They are metals,
metalloids, non-metals and inert gases.

c. d-block elements

When the last electron of an element enters into d-subshell, it is known as d-block
elements. The elements belonging to group IB, IIB, IIIB, IVB, VB, VIB, VIIB
and VIII belong to d-bock. They are also known as transitional elements. They
are placed in between s and p blocks. They all are metals with high density, high
melting and boiling point with variable valency.

d. f-block elements

When the last electron of an clement enters f-subshell, it is known as f-block
element. It consists of two series. The first series of 14 elements are known as
lanthanides and second series of 14 elements are known as actinides. These
elements are placed separately at the bottom of the periodic table. They are also
known as inner transitional elements.

units Chemical Reaction

We observe many changes that are taking place around us. Some of them are temporary
or reversible such as melting of ice, while some are permanent and irreversible such as
burning of wood, digestion of food, etc. and such change occur by the combination,
decomposition or displacement of molecules of the matter. This is called chemical
change. Chemical change is represented by chemical reaction.

Chemical reactions

The combination, decomposition or displacement of molecules of matter during
chemical change is called chemical reaction. Various conditions bring about these
changes. The chemical reactions are represented by chemical equation. The
compounds or elements that take part in chemical reaction are called reactant.
They are written at the left side of an arrow that represent a change while the
compound or elements that formed after the chemical change are called product.
They are written at the right side of the arrow.

Reactant ——» Product
Types of chemical reactions

Depending upon the reactants and the products formed after the chemical change,
chemical reactions are of four types. They are:

1. Combination reaction

 

When two or more reactants combine to form a single product, such chemical
reaction is called combination reaction. For example,

Sodium + Chlorine =——» Sodium Chloride
2Na+Cl; —* 2NaCl
Nitrogen + Hydrogen — Ammonia
N2 + 3H, —* 2NH3
Iron + Sulphur ——> Ferrous Sulphide
Fe+S ——> FeS
Carbon + Oxygen ___, — Carbondioxide

2Al+N; ——> 2AIN

Displacement reaction

When an atom or group of atoms in a molecule is replaced by another atom or
group of atoms, such chemical reaction is called displacement reaction.
Displacement reaction is of two types. They are:

Science- 10 104


a.

a. Single displacement reaction
b. Double displacement reaction

Single displacement reaction
When an atom in a molecule is replaced by another atom, such chemical
reaction is called single displacement reaction. For example,

Zinc + Hydrochloric acid > Zinc Chloride + Hydrogen
Zn+2HCl ——> ZnCh + Hp

Here, zine replaces hydrogen from molecules of hydrochloric acid.
Chlorine + Potassium Bromide— Potassium Chloride + Bromine

Ch + 2KBr —* 2KCl+Bnf

Here, chlorine being more reactive replaces bromine from potassium bromide.
Magnesium + Sulphuric acid —> Magnesium Sulphate + Hydrogen

Mg+HSO, ——» MgSO,+ Ht

Here, magnesium replaces hydrogen from sulphuric acid.
Iron + Copper Sulphate — Zinc Sulphate + Copper

Fe + CuSO, ——> FeSOQ4+ Cu}

Here, iron replaces copper from copper sulphate.
Copper + Silver Chloride — Cupric Chloride + Silver

Cu+2AgCl ——» CuCh+2Ag |

Here, copper replaces silver from silver chloride.

. Double displacement reaction

When there is a mutual exchange of atom or group of atoms having similar
charges between two reacting molecules, the chemical reaction is called
double displacement reaction. For example,

Silver Nitrate + Sodium Chloride — Sodium Nitrate + Silver Chloride

Conditions required for chemical reaction

Various conditions are required for various reactions to occur. Some of them are
given below.

1. Simple contact

Some reaction takes place when the reactants are brought in contact. For
example, when sodium is brought in contact with chlorine, chemical reaction
between them take place and gives sodium chloride as the product

2Na + Cl, —» 2NaCl

2. Catalyst

Some reaction takes place only in presence of catalyst such as hydrogen
peroxide (H,0;) decomposes into oxygen and water in presence of manganese
dioxide (MnO,). Catalysts are the chemical substances that do not take part in
chemical reaction but increase or decrease the rate of reaction. The catalyst
that increases the rate of reaction is called positive catalyst and the catalyst
that decreases the rate of reaction is called negative catalyst.

catalyst (MnO;

Pressure
Some chemical reaction especially that of some gases takes place only in
presence of pressure. For example, nitrogen and hydrogen combine to give
ammonia only in presence of 200-900 atmospheric pressure as well as catalyst
(Fe) and promoter (Mo).

4. Solution phase

Some reaction takes place only when the reactants are in solution phase. When
they come in contact in solid or gaseous states there are no chemical reactions
but when they come in contact in solution phase, they undergo chemical
reactions. For example,

NaCl + AgNO; ——» NaNO; + AgCl

5. Light

Some reactions take place when the reactants are exposed to light. For
example, reaction between hydrogen and chlorine is stimulated by light and
they react violently in presence of light to give hydrochloric acid.

Sunlight (bv
sunlight (hy)

6. Heat

Some reactions take place when the reactants are heated. Heat increases the
vibration of molecules in reactants that help them to come in contact and
result into chemical reaction and give the products. For e.g.

2KCIO; A» 2KCI +30,

7. Electricity

Reaction such as electrolysis, electroplating, etc. takes place when electricity pass
through the solution of reactants. For example, water decomposes into hydrogen and
oxygen by the process of electrolysis.

2H,0 = 2H) +O)

Rate of chemical reaction

The rate of chemical reaction is defined as the time taken by the reactant to change
completely into the product. The rate of the reaction may be slow or fast which depends
upon various factors such as temperature, pressure, presence and absence of catalyst,
surface area of the reactant, ete, which are discussed as follows.

1. Temperature

In general, increase in temperature increases the rate of reaction. When the reactants
are heated, the molecules of the reactants vibrate and they come in contact rapidly as
the result, reaction takes place faster.

Science- 10 108


Activity 8.1

Take two beakers, put some oxalic acid crystals. Add about 10m of dilute sulphuric acid
in each, now add about Sml of potassium permanganate in each. Stir both solutions for
some time, They should become pink in colour. Heat one of the solutions upto 60-70°C
with the help of spirit lamp. Observe which solution decolourises first. The solution that
was heated decolourises first while another decolourise after a long time. Increase in
temperature of first solution increases the rate of reaction between oxalic acid, potassium
permanganate and sulphuric acid.

 

2, Pressure

The rate of chemical reaction especially those of gaseous reactants increase when
pressure is applied. In the gaseous reactants the molecules are far away from each
other. When they are given pressure their volume gets squeezed and as a result, the
molecules come closer to each other and collide to give the products.

Catalyst

y

The rate of chemical reaction is increased by the presence of positive catalyst.
Catalysts have special capacity of altering the rate of reaction without taking part in
the reaction. At the end of the reaction, they remain same or unaffected. Catalyst
decreases ot lowers the energy required to break the bond between the molecules of
the reactant thereby increase the rate of chemical reaction.

4, Surface area

The rate of chemical reaction increases when the reactants are used in powdered
form. This is because the surface area of the reactant increases in powdered form and
as a result, they are exposed to other reactant with large surface area. This enhances
the rate of reaction

Activity 8.2

Take two beakers and pour about 20ml of dilute hydrochloric acid in each. Weigh 2gms
zine pieces and 2gms zinc powder, keep them in watch glasses separately. Transfer the
zine pieces into one beaker and zinc powder into another. Stir with glass rod and observe
in which beaker the bubbles come first. Bubbles come from the beaker containing zinc
powder first while bubbles come after a long time from the beaker containing zine pieces.
Powder zine has more surface area than that of zine pieces so the reaction between
powder zinc and hydrochloric acid takes place faster.

Exothermic and endothermic reaction

On the basis of absorption and release of heat energy during chemical reaction, it is of
two types. When heat is absorbed during the chemical reaction, the reaction is termed as
endothermic reaction whereas when heat is released during the chemical reaction, it is
termed as exothermic reaction.

Some examples of endothermic reaction:

Science- 10 109


CaCO; + heat ——» CaO + CO, #

2KCIO; + heat ——> 2KCl+30,4

N.+0:+heat ——» 2No +

Some examples of exothermic reaction:

C+

OQ; —* CO;+ heat

CaO +H;0 — > Ca(OH); +heat

Zn +2HCl —» ZnCl, + H)+ heat

Summary

10.

11.

The combination, decomposition or displacement of molecules of matter during
chemical change is called chemical reaction.

The compounds or elements that take part in chemical reaction are called reactant.
The compound or elements formed after the chemical change are called product.

When two or more reactants combine to form a single product, the chemical reaction
is called combination reaction.

When a single compound or reactant decomposes to give two or more product, the
chemical reaction is called decomposition reaction.

When an atom or group of atoms in a molecule is replaced by another atom or group
of atoms, the chemical reaction is called displacement reaction.

When an atom in a molecule is replaced by another atom, the chemical reaction is
called single displacement reaction.

When there is a mutual exchange of atom or group of atoms between two reacting
molecules, the chemical reaction is called double displacement reaction.

When acid reacts with base, it gives salt and water. This reaction is known as acid
base reaction.

Catalysts are the chemical substances that do not take part in chemical reaction as
reactant but increase or decrease the rate of reaction.

The rate of the reaction depends upon various factors such as temperature, pressure,
presence and absence of catalyst surface area of the reactant, ete.

Unit 9 Acid, Base and Salt

In our daily lives, we have been using acids, bases and salts for various purposes. We are
using them in our food, agriculture, medicines and industries. For example, we have acids
such as citric acid, tartaric acid, ascorbic acid, etc. present in different fruits and
vegetables. Vinegar is an essential ingredient in our pickles and we cannot imagine our
food without common salts (sodium chloride). Similarly, caustic soda in the manufacture
of soap, aluminium hydroxide is used as medicine for acidity and calcium hydroxide is
used in order to neutralize the acidity of soil. Therefore, acids, bases and salts are playing
very important role in our daily lives. In this unit, we shall study about them in detail.

Acids

The word acid is derived from the Latin word “acidus” which means sour. Acids are sour
in taste such as citric acid, ascorbic acid, tartaric acid, But acids used in laboratory such
as hydrochloric acid, nitric acid and sulphuric acid cannot be tasted.

In general acids may be defined as the compound which when dissolved in water gives
hydrogen ions (H" ions) For example,

Hydrochloric acid
HCl = H'+cr
Sulphuric Acid
H)SO, = 2H" + SOs
Nitric Acid

HNO; = H’ +NO;"

Acetic Acid

 

CH;COOH = CH;COO"+ H*

Carbonic Acid

H,CO; = 2H’ + CO;

Types of Acids

On the basis of their origin, acids are of two types. They are:
1. Organic acids

Acids that are found in animals and plants are known as organic acids. For example,

Science- 10 114


formic acid is found in ant bites, citric acid in lemons, tartaric acid and ascorbic acid
are found in fruits and vegetables.

Inorganic acids

Acids that are prepared in laboratory are known as inorganic acids. For example,
hydrochloric acid, nitric acid and sulphuric acid. They are comparatively stronger
than organic acid,

Activity 9.1

Take a test tube and place piece of magnesium or limestone. Pour some dilute
Hydrochloric acid and observe. Now take another test tube and place a piece of
magnesium or limestone, pour some acetic acid and observe. In which test tube does the
bubble come first? Discuss with your friends and the teacher.

On the basis of concentration of hydrogen ions produced during ionization, they are
further divided into two types. They are:

1.

Strong Acids

‘Acids which produce more hydrogen ions during ionization in aqueous phase are
known as strong acids. Hydrochloric acid, nitric acid and sulphuric acid are some of
the example of strong acid which undergo complete dissociation to produce more
hydrogen ions in aqueous solution.

2. Weak Acids

Acids which produce less hydrogen ions during ionization in aqueous phase are
known as weak acids. Carbonic acid and acetic acid are some of the examples of
weak acid. They do not undergo complete dissociation and hence produce only less
amount of hydrogen ions in aqueous solution.

Characteristics of acids

1. Most of the acids are sour in taste.
2. Strong acids are corrosive in nature.

3. Acids change the colour of indicators as given in the table below

 

Red litmus Blue litmus Methyl orange | Phenolphthalein

Bases

Bases are metallic oxides. Some bases are soluble in water to give metal hydroxides
known as alkali. So alkalis are also bases but all the bases are not alkalis. Thus
collectively, bases are metal oxides and hydroxides that give salt and water when reacted

with acids. Alkalis give hydroxide ions (OH) when dissolved in water.

Magnesium oxide (MgO), Calcium oxide (CaO), Sodium oxide (Na;O), Sodium
hydroxides (NaOH), Potassium hydroxide (KOH) and Calcium hydroxide (Ca(OH)2) are

some examples of bases.

Bases are generally formed by the reaction between reactive metals and oxygen. For

example,

2Mg+0, —» 2MgO

116


4K +0; —» 2K,0

Bases are also formed when metal carbonates decompose. Such as,
A
CaCO; ——> Ca0+CO;

Naco, —*- Na0+C0,

Some bases dissolve in water to give alkalis. For example,

Na,O + HO ———> 2NaOH

K,0 + H,0 ———» 2KOH

Al,O;+ H,O0 ———> 2AI(OH)

CaO +H,;0 ——> Ca(OH),

These alkalis ionize to give hydroxyl ion in aqueous phase. For example,
NaOH = Na’ + OH

KOH = K* + OH

Types of bases

On the basis of amount of hydroxy! ions released when dissolved in water, bases are
classified into two types:

1. Strong bases

Bases that undergo complete dissociation and give more hydroxide ions when
dissolved in water are known as strong base. For e.g. Sodium hydroxide (NaOH),
Potassium hydroxide (KOH).

2. Weak bases

Bases that undergo only partial dissociation and give less amount of hydroxide ions
when dissolved in water are known as weak bases. For e.g. Ammonium
hydroxide(NH,OH) and Calcium hydroxide (Ca(OH),).

Salts

Salts are defined as the compound formed by the partial or complete displacement of one
or more hydrogen atom of an acid by metals or any other positive radical

In any salt, there is positive radical that comes from base known as basic radical and a
negative radical that comes from acid known as acidic radical

NaOH +HCl ——+NaCl +H,0 (Complete displacement)
KOH +H,SO, ——> KHSO,+HL0 (Partial displacement)

Here, in sodium chloride Na’ radical comes from base so it is a basic radical while Cl”
radical comes from acid so it is an acidic radical.

Types of salts
Salts are of four types.
1, Neutral salts

The salts formed by the complete displacement of hydrogen from acids are
neutral in nature. They are called neutral salts.

KOH+HCl —+ KCI+H,0
NaOH + H:SO, ——> Na)SO, + H20

KOH + HNO; ——* KNO; +H,0

2. Acidic salts

NaOH +Such salts are called acidic salts. These types of salts are also formed
by the reaction between strong acid and weak base.

NaOH + H,SO, ——» NaHSO, + H;,0
Here, H, partially displaced from H:SO..

H,SO, + 2NH;OH ——® (NH4)2SO, + H:0

acids are basic in nature. Such salts are called basic salts.

Strong base + weak acid — basic salt + water

Hydrated salts

The hydrated salts are the salts that have certain number of water molecules
associated with it, These water molecules are called the water of
crystallization or water of hydration. For example,

Sodium sulphate decahydrate, Na,SO,10H,O

Epsom salt (Magnesium sulphate heptahydrate), MgSO,7H;0

Blue vitriol (Copper II sulphate pentahydrate), CuSO.5H,O

White vitriol (Zinc sulphate heptahydrated), Zn$O,7H,0
Characteristics of salts

1. Common salt is salty in taste but most of the salts are bitter and some are
tasteless.

2. Most of the salts are white in colour and some are coloured, especially the salt
of Cu, Fe, Mn, Cr, etc. are coloured.

Science- 10 120


3. Salts are generally soluble in water and undergo electrolysis to give respective
cations and anions.

4. The salts formed by strong acid and strong base or weak acid and weak base
are neutral to all the indicator while salts formed by strong acid and weak base
are acidic in nature and those formed by strong base and weak acid are basic
in nature so they behave with indicator accordingly

Neutralization reaction

When acid reacts with base, acid neutralizes basic nature of base and base neutralizes
acidic nature of acid and produce neutral salt and water. This reaction is known as
neutralization reaction. For example,

NaOH + HCl ————* NaCl + H,0

KOH + HNO; ————» _ KNO; + H,0

Ca(OH)2+ H,SO, ————> CaSO,+ H,0

Application of neutralization reaction

1.

Farmers apply lime to reduce the acidity of soil, If the soil is basic, it is treated
with composts made of rotting vegetables or leaves. The acidic gas from the
decomposition of compost neutralizes the alkalis in basic soil

  

2. We use antacid [Mg(OH), ] to get relief from hyperacidity (gastritis).
Wasp stings are alkaline in nature so vinegar is used to cure it

4. Bee stings and ant bites are acidic in nature. So soap, baking powder etc. are
applied to cure it.

5. Decaying food particles in our teeth produce acid that causes tooth decay. Using
toothpaste during brushing helps to neutralize the acid since toothpaste is alkaline
in nature.

Summary

1. Acids are defined as the compound which when dissolved in water gives
hydrogen ions.

2. Bases are metallic oxides. Some bases are soluble in water to give metal
hydroxides known as alkali.

3. When acid reacts with base, acid neutralizes basic nature of base and base

neutralizes acidic nature of acid to produce neutral compounds salt and
water. This reaction is known as neutralization reaction.

Unit 10 Some Gases

Carbon dioxide
Molecular formula = CO,
Molecular weight = 44

Van Helmont prepared carbon dioxide for the first time around 1640AD by burning
wood. In 1755 AD, Joseph Black prepared carbon dioxide by buming magnesium
carbonate. Late in 1783 AD, Lavoisier studied its properties.

Occurrence

Carbon dioxide occurs in free and combined state in nature. It is present in about 0.03%
of atmosphere. Similarly, it is produced when wood, coal, fuel, ete. burn. It is also
produced during respiration of plants and animals and the decomposition of metal
carbonates and bicarbonates such as calcium carbonate and magnesium carbonate.

Laboratory preparation of carbon dioxide

Theory

Carbon dioxide is prepared in laboratory by the reaction between marble pieces (CaCOs)
and dilute hydrochloric acid.

CaCO;+2HCl ——*> CaCl, +H,0 + CO,*#

Apparatus required

1. Woult’s bottle
2. Thistle funnel tie
3. Gas jar funel
4. Delivery tube
5. Cork | asa
Chemicals required
~CO, ges

  

1. Dilute hydrochloric acid (HCI) Bs

2. Marble pieces (CaCO;) :
Procedure

a. Wash and fit the apparatus as shown in the diagram.

b. Pour dilute hydrochloric acid from the thistle funnel into the woulf’s bottle
containing marble chips.

c. Brisk effervescence takes place due to the reaction between marble chips and
dilute hydrochloric acid.

Science- 10 125


d. The carbon dioxide so formed in collected in a gas jar by the upward
displacement of air since CO) is heavier than air.

Precaution

* Thistle funnel should be dipped into hydrochloric acid in order to prevent the
escaping of CO, formed through it.

* Delivery tube should be above the solution for passing the CO) so formed into
the end of gas jar.

* The apparatus should be airtight.
© The gas should be collected by upward displacement of air.
Other methods of preparation of carbon dioxide

Manufacture of carbon dioxide

When carbon dioxide is required in big amount, it is manufactured by heating calcium
carbonate in big furnace. The carbon dioxide so formed is used for industrial purpose and
the by-product calcium oxide is used for white washing and it is also used to prepare
limewater.

taste.

It turns wet blue litmus into red colour.
When it is cooled upto -78°C it tums into a white solid ice known as dry ice.

It is not a poisonous gas but it may cause the loss of consciousness due to
increase in concentration of carbon dioxide in the body.

Activity 10.2

Study and observe carefully a fire extinguisher. Learn how to use it and

Carbon dioxide is used in the drinks such as soda water, soft drinks, beer, etc. to
have sour taste.

Itis used as a raw material for the photosynthesis by the green plants.
tis used in the manufacture of urea and washing soda (Na;CO;).

Itis used to make dry ice which is used to preserve fruits, fish, and meat products
for a long time

It is also used as fire extinguisher. It does not itself react to extinguish the flame
but helps to reduce the flame by displacing oxygen supply since it is heavier gas.

Itis also used in the manufacture of sugar from sugar cane. Carbon dioxide along
with calcium hydroxide helps to remove colour, gum and amino acids impurities

The mixture of 95% oxygen and 5% carbon dioxide gas is known as carbogen. It
is used as medicine for pneumonia.

 

Ammonia

Molecular formula = NH3

Molecular weight = 17

Science- 10 128


Ammonia is a gas containing one nitrogen and three hydrogen atoms. It was first prepared
by Antoine Lavoisier. He heated ammonium chloride and ammonia gas was liberated.
Later on, Bertrelot and Davy studied about its composition.

Occurrence

Ammonia occurs in nature in trace amount. It is produced from the nitrogenous animals
and vegetable matter. Ammonia and ammonium salts are also formed in small quantities
in rainwater whereas ammonium chloride and ammonium sulphate are found in vole:
areas.

 

Laboratory preparation of Ammonia
Theory

Ammonia is prepared in laboratory by heating the finely crushed mixture of dry solid
ammonium chloride and calcium hydroxide in the ratio 2:1.

Manufacture of ammonia gas

‘Ammonia is manufactured in large scale by combination reaction of nitrogen from the air
with hydrogen derived mainly from natural gas (methane). The process is also known as
Haber’s synthesis of ammonia. The reaction is reversible and exothermic. Fine powder of
iron is used as catalyst and molybdenum is used as promoter. Similarly, 400-500°C heat
is provided and 200-900 atmospheric pressure is applied for the reaction to take place.
500°C, Fe/Mo
No+ 3H,» 2NH+ heat
200-900 atm
Therefore, for the better yield of ammonia, following conditions should be met,
1. Temperature should be maintained between 400-500°C.
2. Atmospheric pressure of 200-900 atmosphere should be applied.
3. Fine powder of iron should be used as catalyst.
4,

Traces of Molybdenum should be used as promoter which enhances the
function of catalyst.

5, Reactants i.e. nitrogen and hydrogen should be used in 1:3 ratio.

Ammonia is a colourless and tasteless gas with pungent smell.

It is lighter than air.

It is neither combustible nor a supporter of combustion.

It is basic in nature.

Its liquifies at -33.4°C and solidifies at -78°C.

Uses

1. Ammonia is best used in fertilizer industries for the manufacture of chemical
fertilizers like urea, ammonium sulphate, ammonium nitrate, ammonium chloride
etc.

2. It is also used in the manufacture of nitric acid, dyes and alkalis.

3. It is also used in the manufacture of pharmaceuticals such as sulfa drugs,
vitamins and cosmetics.

4, It is used in the synthetic textile fibers such as nylon, rayon and acrylics.

The petroleum industry utilizes ammonia in neutralizing the acid constituents of
crude oil and for the protection of various equipment from corrosion.

It is also used in water and wastewater treatment such as pH control.
It's used as the developing agent in white printing, blue printing of maps.

It is used as cleansing agent for removing oil and grease.

. The food and beverages industry uses ammonia as a source of nitrogen for yeast
and microorganisms.

highly soluble in water.

9. Ammonia is manufactured in large scale by combination reaction of nitrogen from
the air with hydrogen derived mainly from natural gas (methane). The process is also
known as Haber’s synthesis.

Unit 11 Metals

We have been using metals since ancient times for different purposes. Metals are being
used in different sectors such as industries, households, transportation, weapon making,
jewelleries, electric wires, coins, etc. Metals are good conductor of heat and electricity.
They are malleable and ductile. They are shiny and lustrous. Properties as such made
metals even more useful.

 

Metals are generally present as minerals or ores. Minerals are those naturally occurring
substances that contain metals in more or less amount, whereas ores are the minerals from
which metals can be extracted conveniently and profitably. In this unit, we shall study
some useful metals in detail.

Some important metals

It is a magnetic substance but turns non magnetic above 770°C.

Its melting point is about 1500°C while its boiling point is about 2500°C.

Uses of iron
Iron is used in the construction of machine parts, building materials such as rod,

Physical properties

Uses of copper

It is used for the preparation of useful alloys such as bronze , brass (Cu + Zn) ,
german silver (Cu + Zn + Ni), etc.

It is used for electroplating and electrotyping.
It is also used to make coins, ornaments and jewellery.
Copper salts are used as insecticides, fungicides, germicides, dyes and pigments.

It is also used in electronics and related devices.

Unit12 Hydrocarbon and its Compounds

As the name suggests, hydrocarbons are the compounds formed by the
combination of hydrogen and carbons. The main source of hydrocarbons is
petroleum and natural gases. In some hydrocarbons, some foreign elements such
as oxygen, nitrogen, sulfur, etc. may be present. There are mainly two types of
hydrocarbons. They are,

1. Saturated hydrocarbon
2. Unsaturated hydrocarbon
1. Saturated hydrocarbon

The hydrocarbons in which carbon atoms are connected by a single covalent
bond are known as saturated hydrocarbons. They are called alkanes. Their
molecular formula is written by the formula C,H»:2 , where n represents the
number of carbon. For example, Methane (CH,), Ethane (C)H,), Propane
(CsHs), ete.

2. Unsaturated hydrocarbon

The hydrocarbons in which carbon atoms are connected by two or three
(double or triple) covalent bonds are known as unsaturated hydrocarbons. The
hydrocarbons in which carbon atoms are connected by double covalent bond
are known as alkenes. Their molecular formula is written by the formula
Cutan where n represents the number of carbon. For example Ethene (C:H:) ,
Propene (CsH,), Butene (C)Hs), ete.

They hydrocarbon in which carbon atoms are connected by triple covalent bonds
are known as alkynes. Their molecular formulas are written by the formula C,H2n.
2 where n is the number of carbon atoms. For example, Ethyne (CH,), Propyne
(C3H.), Butyne (CsHs) ete.

TUPAC system of nomenclature

The full form of IUPAC is International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry
This is an organization which suggests us the system of nomenclature on naming
of hydrocarbon. According to IUPAC system of nomenclature, hydrocarbons are
named with the formula: prefix + word root + suffix. But in class ten, we don’t
study about the hydrocarbons that have prefix. So, for class ten, we use the
formula: word root + suffix. Here, word root shows the number of carbon and the
suffix shows the number of carbon-carbon bond in any hydrocarbon.

 

Depending upon the number carbon atom following word roots are used.

Functional group

An atom or group of atoms that determine the physical and chemical properties of any
hydrocarbon is known as functional group. For example, hydroxyl group (— OH) is the
functional group of alcohol and ether group (— O — )is the functional group of ether. It
is attached to the alkyl radical and forms organic compound called derivative. For
example

 

 

 

Alkane Alkyl radical | Functional | noivative | TUPAC) Common

Homologous series

  

A series of organic compounds having similar functional group that can be represented by
same general formula are called homologus series. The compounds in homologus series
are arranged in increasing order of carbon i.e. each successive member of homologus
series differ by CH) group. All the members of homologus series can be prepared by
similar methods. For example,

Homologus series of alkane:

[By common formula CyHon+2]

 

1. CHy Methane
2. CH; CH; Ethane
3. CH; CH;CH; Propane
4. CH3CHz CH) CHs Butane
5. CH; CH) CH; CH; CH; Pentane
Homologus series of alcohol:
1, CH;OH Methanol
2. CHyCH,OH Ethanol
3. CHsCH;CH,OH Propanol
4 CH,CH,0H Butanol
5. CH;CH;CH,CH;CH,OH Pentanol

Isomers and isomerism

The organic compounds having similar molecular formula but different structural formula
are called isomers. The properties of organic compounds to exist in two or more isomers
are known as isomerism. Hydrocarbons having four or more carbons are capable of
forming isomers or they show isomerism. For example,

Types of alcohol

Depending upon the number of hydroxyl group present in alcohol, it is of three types.

The alcohol having only one hydroxyl group (—OH) is called monohydric alcohol. It
is formed by replacing one hydrogen of alkane by hydroxyl group. For example,
methyl alcohol (CH;OF), ethyl alcohol (C2HsOH ), propyl alcohol (C3H;OH ).

Glucose

The word glucose was derived from “glukus” which means sweet. Glucose is a simple
sugar with the molecular formula C,H;3,. It is white powder, which is easily soluble in
water. It is formed during photosynthesis from water and carbon dioxide in presence of
sunlight and chlorophyll whereas just reverse process occurs during respiration. The
glucose is broken down into water, carbon dioxide and energy in animals and plants
during cellular respiration.

Ghucose can be obtained by hydrolysis of carbohydrates such as milk, sugar, cellulose
found in different fruits and food. Glucose is an important source of energy in most
organisms from bacteria to humans. But when it is present in high and low amount in
blood, it is harmful in either case.

with toothpick and make the models of different hydrocarbon. Show it to your teacher.

Summary

1. Hydrocarbons are the compounds formed by the combination of hydrogen and
carbons.

2. The hydrocarbons in which carbon atoms are connected by a single covalent
bond are known as saturated hydrocarbons.

3. The hydrocarbons in which carbon atoms are connected by two or three (double
or triple) covalent bonds are known as unsaturated hydrocarbons.

4. The full form of IUPAC is International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry.

5. An atom or group of atoms that determines the physical and chemical properties
of any hydrocarbon is known as functional group.

6. A series of organic compounds having similar functional group that can be
represented by same general formula are called homolgus series.

7. The organic compounds having similar molecular formula but different structural
formula are called isomers.

8. The properties of organic compounds to exist in two or more isomers are known
as isomerism.

Science- 10 154


9. Organic compounds formed by replacing one or more hydrogen of alkane by
hydroxyl group (—OH group) are known as alcohols.

10. Glycerol is a trihydric alcohol formed by the replacement of three hydrogen
atoms of propane by three hydroxyl groups

11. Glucose can be obtained by hydrolysis of carbohydrates such as milk, sugar,
cellulose found in different fruits and food.

Science- 10 187


unit13 Materials Used in Daily Life

Everything around us is ultimately made up of chemicals be it living or non living
things, natural or man made. Chemistry is very important because it helps us to know
the composition, structures and changes of matter. In our every day lives, various
products made up of chemicals are being used in various forms. These products not
only fulfill basic needs of humans but also makes their lives easier, comfortable and
more luxurious. For example, the chemical substances such as cement, plastics,
fertilizers, pesticides, glass, soap and detergents, fibres, medicines and dyes are the
integral part of our lives without which our lives become almost impossible. In this
unit we study about the composition and uses of such substances.
Cement
Cement is a fine gray powder of calcium silicate and calcium aluminate that is
prepared by calcium carbonate (CaCO;) and special type of clay (Al,O;Si0,). It sets
into hard rock like form that binds other materials together during construction of
infrastructures.
Manufacture of cement
The raw materials that are used for the manufacture of cement are
1. Lime stone (CaCO,)
2. Special type of clay (Al,03 Si0;).
3. Gypsum (CaSO,.2H,0)
The manufacture of cement from above mentioned raw materials involves following
steps,
1. Crushing and grinding
The big lumps of limestone and the special type of clay are first crushed and
grounded into fine powder separately.
2, Slurry formation
The powder of limestone and clay is mixed in 2:1 ratio along with water to make
semi liquid mixture known as cement slurry.
3. Heating of slurry
The slurry thus formed is fed into the rotary kiln where it is heated at the
temperature of about 1400°C to 1600°C
In the kiln limestone decomposes into calcium oxide and carbon dioxide. The
calcium oxide so formed combines with alumina (Al;03) and silica (SiO2) to
form calcium aluminate and calcium silicate respectively. As a result, small pea
sized red balls of cements are formed which are known as cement clinkers.

158


Chemical composition of cement clinkers are calcium silicate and calcium
aluminate.

2CaCO; + Al,0,Si0, “4, Ca$iOs + CaAlOs +2 CO) +
1400-1600°C
4. Mixing with gypsum
To slow down the setting time of cement and improve its quality, the cement
clinkers are mixed with 2-3% of hydrated calcium sulphate known as gypsum.
5. Final grinding

The mixture is again ground into fine powder. This fine powder is finally called

cement, which is packed into airtight bags and stored in dry place to protect it

from moisture.
Uses

* Cement is used to make cement mortar, which is a mixture of cement, sand
and water, which is used for building and plastering of walls.

© It is used to make concrete, which is a mixture of cement, sand, water, and
gravel, which is used for roofing, flooring and making pillars of buildings.

* It is used to make reinforced cement concrete (RCC) which is a mixture of
cement, sand, water and gravel along with iron framework inside which is
used for making pillars, roofs, floors of building and bridges.

Glass

Glass is hard, transparent, amorphous metallic silicate of different metals. Glass,
though seems to be solid, the molecules of glass are found moving very slowly like
that occurs in extremely cold liquid so it is also called a super cooled liquid. That is
why the window pane of old houses got thick at the bottom while thin at the top. The
main raw material or component of glass is silica found in sand.

Depending upon the components and chemical mixed in it, glass is of different types.
Some of them are given below.

1.

Quartz glass
When pure silica is strongly heated at 1600°C and allowed to cool slowly, it turns
into quartz glass.

160°C
Pure silica —— Quartz glass
It differs from other glasses in not containing any other ingredients except silica.
Therefore, it is superior to other types of glasses due to its purity. For these
reasons, it is used to make laboratory equipment, lenses, gems, basins, crucibles,
electric tools, etc.

159


Water glass

When a mixture of silica (SiO,) and sodium carbonate (Na,COs) or potassium
carbonate (K,COs) is heated at 800°C water glass is formed which is sodium
silicate.

A

Potassium silicate
It is soluble in water, so it is called water glass. Water glass is used for making
fireproof materials, adhesive and chemical garden. A chemical garden is an
experiment in chemistry normally performed by adding metal salts such as
copper sulphate or cobalt (II) chloride to an aqueous solution of water glass. This
results in growth of plant like forms in minutes to hours

 

Ordinary glass or soda glass
The glass formed by fusing a mixture of 50% silica, 15% sodium carbonate, 10%
calcium carbonate and 25% glass pieces at 850°C in a special type of tank
furnace is ordinary or soda glass. It is the most common type of glass. The
mixture melts into a viscous liquid, which is poured into moulds or blown with
iron pipe to make different glasswares. The almost ready glasswares are cooled
slowly. This process is known as annealing. Annealing makes the glassware
resistant to temperature fluctuation. By this process, glasswares do not break
easily when something hot or cold is put in it.

Glass pieces

Ordinary glass is used for making bottles, light bulbs, window panes and several
other useful objects. It is also known as soft glass.
Hard glass or potash lime glass
The glass formed by fusing a mixture of potassium carbonate, calcium carbonate
and silica is called hard glass or potash lime glass. It has high melting point and
can withstand high temperature.

to the chemicals and heat.

Ceramics

Ceramics is special type of clay used for pottery, bricks kitchen wares, ete.
Chemically, it is a hydrated aluminium silicate (Al,O3.SiO,.2H,0). It may have
other substances such as magnesium, carbonates, oxides of manganese, limestone
and iron. The pure white clay is also known as kaolin or china clay.

Production of different materials from ceramics

The clay is crushed, ground and sieved. It is then mixed with water to make paste.
Then, it is given different shapes on a potter’s wheel. Then, it is dried and heated in
kilns at high temperature. Heating makes the objects hard and strong and make them
durable but still is porous and absorbs water. So, now it is coated with tin oxide or
lead oxide and then again heated. This process is called glazing. Glazing makes the
object waterproof and beautiful. It is also painted with metal salts and different
designs to make it more attractive.

Ceramics can withstand high temperatures. It is resistant to chemicals, poor
conductors of heat and electricity and is hard and brittle. Because of all these
properties, they are widely used for following purposes.

161


1. Ceramics are used to make different kitchen wares such as cup, plates, bowls and
dishes,

2. Being poor conductor of electricity, they are used as insulators in electrical
appliances.

3. They are used to make artificial teeth and bone joints

4. They are also used to make bathroom tiles, sinks, commodes, roof tiles and
bricks.

Polymers and Plastics

Polymers is an extremely large molecule consisting of a chain or network of small

molecules called monomers. The process of forming polymer by large number of

monomers is known as polymerization, For example, polyethene is a polymer

obtained by joining large number of ethene (CH, = CH). Similarly, Vinyl chlorides

are monomer units of polymer PVC (Polyvinyl chloride)

Polymers may be natural such as protein, starch, cellulose, silk, wool, rubber or

synthetic such as plastics, nylon and terylene.

Plastics

The word plastics comes from the greek word ‘plastikos’ which means capable of

being moulded. So, plastics means a manmade polymer which can be moulded to any

shape when they are warm. The starting monomers for plastics are obtained from

crude oil and small quantities other substances often used to improve performances

or to reduce cost.

Types of plastics

Plastics are of two types

1. Thermoplasties

2. Thermosetting plastics

1, Thermoplasties
Plastics which becomes soft on heating and hard on cooling are known as
thermoplastics. The monomers in this type of plastics are connected via straight
chain, They can be moulded again and again into different shapes. For example,
polycthene, polyvinyl chloride (PVC), polystyrene or polyster, etc.

162

Thermosetting plastics

Plastics which are hard and strong and are resistant to heat is known as
thermosetting plastics. The monomers in thermoplastic setting plastics are
arranged in cross linkage, which cannot be broken down by heating. So, they
cannot be moulded again and again. For example, bakellite, melamine, ete.

ete.

 

Advantages of plastics
1. They can be easily shaped and moulded.

2. They do not get rot, rust. They are unaffected by chemicals. So they are very
durable.

3. They are cheap and light.

4. They can be used as insulators for heat and electricity.

5. They can be recycled (except thermosetting plastics).

Disadvantages of plastics

1. They are non biodegradable and cause pollution.

2. They produce poisonous gases and smoke when burnt.

3. They easily catch fire.

4. They do not look as good as wood, stone or metals.

163

 


Soap

Soap is substance used for cleaning purposes. Chemically, it is a salt of fatty acids
that is capable of carrying away dirt along with water. Soaps are obtained by treating
vegetable or animal oils and fats with a strong base such as sodium hydroxide or
potassium hydroxide in an aqueous solution. A few drops of sodium chloride are
added in order to increase precipitation. The process of making soap is known as
saponification.

An example of soap is Sodium Stearate: NtOOCR or NaCOOC\7H3;.
C3H;(OOCR); + 3NaOH =——® 3NaOOCR + C3H;(OH);

fats/oils alkali soap glycerol
Here, R represents alkyl radical of long chain hydrocarbons.

R=Ci;Hss = Stearate

R=CidHss = Oleate

R=CjsH3; = Palmitate

When soap is used for cleaning purposes in hard water, it reacts with the salt of
calcium and magnesium present in hard water to form a brown layer on the clothes

known as scum. So, soaps are not very good for cleaning purpose in hard water. But
since soaps are biodegradable, they do not cause any pollution.

Detergents

Detergents are synthetic organic compounds obtained from hydrocarbon extracted
from petroleum such as alkyl benzene sulphonate. Therefore, detergents are the
sodium salt of long chain benzene sulphonic acid. Detergents are suitable for
cleaning purposes in hard water, they do not react with calcium and magnesium salt
of hard water so do not form any scum. Detergents are cheaper than soaps because
they are formed from the byproduct of petroleum. Therefore, detergents are also
known as soapless soap as they have cleansing action like soap but are formed by the
chemicals that are different from soap. One example of detergent is Sodium lauryl
sulphate (C;2H2sSO,-Na’).

But most of the detergents are non-biodegradable so they are not broken down into
simpler and harmless form by the microorganism. Thus, they cause chemical
pollution.

Fertilizers

Plants absorb the nutrients from the soil. Continuous growing of crops leads to the
consumption of a lot of nutrients from the soil. Consequently, the nutrients and
minerals in the soil decrease. Beside these natural processes such as rain and soil
erosion also decreases the nutrients in soil. As a result, soil fertility is decreased and
the production or yield becomes poor. The addition of fertilizer provides essential
elements to the growing plants as well as nourishes the microorganism

Thus, the fertilizers are the substances that are added to the soil to replenish the
nutrients consumed by growing plants. A good fertilizer should be easily soluble in
water and it should not leave any harmful impact to the soil.

Types of fertilizers
There are two types of fertilizers

1. Organic fertilizers

2. Inorganic or chemical fertilizers
1. Organic fertilizers

The fertilizers that are formed by the decomposition of dead plants, dead
animals, and animal wastes are known as organic fertilizers. They are also
known as composts. The green manure and animal manure are the examples of
compost.

When the green plants are grown in the land are ploughed back into the soil.
They decay in the soil and supply nutrients. This type of fertilizer is called green
manure. Green manure is highly nutritive so adds a layer of humus in the soil.
Cow dungs, poultry droppings can directly be used in the soil in order to increase
the fertility of soil.

Other way of using organic fertilizer is by collecting the organic waste from
kitchen in a bin or pit and let them decompose by microorganism to give
compost. The compost so formed is collected and sprinkled in the soil.Organic
fertilizers are very good in providing organic matter to the soil. They do not
cause any environmental pollution. They retain the water in the soil and also
prevent the soil damage. But they are unable to provide inorganic matter such as
nitrogen, phosphorus and potassium sufficiently to the soil.

165


2. Inorganic or chemical fertilizers

The man made chemical compounds which when added to the soil, provide the
essential nutrients required for the growth of soil are known as inorganic or
chemical fertilizers. The chief elements necessary for the growth of plants are
nitrogen, potassium and phosphorus.

Insecticides

Man made poisonous chemicals that are used for destroying or killing harmful
insects are known as insecticides. Generally, most insecticides are poisonous and
non-biodegradable. They are toxic to humans and animals. So, sincere precaution
should be taken during their application. There is both advantages and disadvantages
of using insecticides. Disadvantages are mostly long term and severe so insecticides
should be used as less as possible. Insecticides with very minimal residual effect
should be used. Some examples of insecticides are,

1. Malathion

Parathion

Dichloro dephenyl trichloro ethane (DDT)
Benzene hexa chloride (BHC)

Dialdrin

Aldrin

Baygon

eI aA RYN

Turmic
Classification of insecticides
1. Organic insecticides

a. Organochlorine
DDT, BHC, Aldrin, Dialdrin, Methoxychlor

b. Organophosphorous
Malathion, Parathion, Phosdrin

c. Carbamate : Baygon,Turmic
2. Inorganic insecticides
Calcium Arsenate, Fluorides, Lead arsenate, Lime sulphur
Advantages of insecticides

1. They control harmful insects destroying all the stage of life cycle and
increase crop production.

2. They also help in disease control.

166


Disadvantages of insecticides
1. The continuous use of insecticides makes the insects more resistant to them.
2. Insecticides may kill useful insects also thereby disturbing the ecosystem.

3. If no precaution is taken, farmers may suffer from its harmful effect on
health,

4. Consumption of fruits and vegetables with insecticides residue may lead to
serious diseases like cancer to humans.

Fibres

Fibres are thread like strong materials used for making clothes, nets, ropes, etc. There
are two types of fibres. They are:

1, Natural fibres

Fibres that are obtained from animals and plants are called natural fibres such as
cotton from cotton plants, silk from silkworm, wool from sheep, rabbit, etc.
Natural fibres have some good quality such as retaining body heat, not catching
fire, absorb perspiration, and beautiful and classic in looks. However, these are
not available abundantly. There are some other disadvantages as well which are
as follows:

a, They shrink when washed with water.

b. Insects easily attack them.

c. They are very expensive.

d. They absorb a lot of water so difficult to get dried.
e.

. They do not retain crease on ironing.
Artificial fibres

rp

The man made fibres are called artificial fibres. They are of two types.
a, Synthetic fibres

Man made fibres made by chemical processes is called synthetic fibres. For
example, nylon, terylene, terywool, etc.

-

Reeyeled fibres

These are the man made fibres by recycling the natural fibres. For example,
Rayon is prepared by cotton cellulose and wood pulp. It looks like silk and is
also called “artificial silk”.

Artificial fibres are strong and more convenient. They do not shrink on washing.
They are not attacked by insects. They are easy to wash and dry up quickly.
Moreover, they are cheap and can be manufactured as much as required. This
way synthetic fibres overcome all the demerits of natural fibres.

167


Chemical Pollution

The pollution caused by excessive use and improper disposal of chemicals is called
chemical pollution. Chemical used in different forms such as fertilizer, insecticides,
soaps, detergents, plastics, etc are polluting the environment. Increase in population
and modemization increases demands of different chemicals in the form of various
products. Their improper use and disposal are contaminating the environment more
seriously these days.

Causes of chemical pollution
1. Use of chemical fertilizer and insecticides

It is said that use of chemical fertilizer and insecticides is necessary evil. It is
necessary for increasing the production of crops to meet the demand of
increasing population but at the same time, it is causing different harmful effects
such as air, soil and water pollution. Chemical fertilizer and insecticides are the
chief chemical pollutants, which not only decreasing the air, soil and water
quality but also spreading, eye, lung diseases to humans.

 

 

Besides, mixing of chemical fertilizer in water sources enhances the over growth
of algae which absorb oxygen in water and causes death of fish and other aquatic
life by the lack of oxygen content in water

2. Household, automobile and industrial wastes

The household wastes such as unwanted food, vegetables, clothes, detergents, ete,

are thrown wherever possible, so household wastes are one of the most common
source of pollution. Similarly, industries and vehicles such as car, buses,
motorbikes, trucks etc., emit gases such as carbon monoxides, carbon dioxide,
sulphur dioxide, sulphur trioxides, etc. These are the major gases that cause air
pollution. And also these gases combined with water to form acids such as
carbonic acid, sulphuric acid, nitric acid, etc. come down as acid rain.

 

The smoke coming out from vehicles also combines with fog to form smog,
which causes poor visibility, and disturb road and air traffic.

3. Plastics

Plastics are the major constituents of solid wastes. It is non biodegradable and
remain in the environment for a long time. When burned also they produce toxic
gases. So plastics are another big problem that cause chemical pollution.

4. Colouring materials of food

Different food colours are used to make the food attractive but most of the food
colours are made up of chemicals so they harm human health directly or
indirectly causing different diseases.

168


5. Metallic particles

Different metallic particles such as lead, chromium, arsenic, mercury, cadmium,
etc. get mixed with air via different sources such as petroleum products, metallic
particles used materials like mercury vapour filled tube lights, etc. Exhaust of
automobile gives out lead oxide when human inhale it, it causes severe effect on
human health. When metals such as arsenic get mixed with water, it kills aquatic
animals and causes different skin diseases to humans.

6. Dyes

Dyes are released out from textiles and leather industries, caustic soda, etc. which
causes water pollution when mixed in water resources.

 

Control of chemical pollution

1. Public awareness should be raised.
Population growth and unplanned urbanization should be controlled.
Use of insecticides and chemical fertilizer should be limited.

Proper monitoring mechanism should be developed.

yeep

Biodegradable and non-biodegradable waste should be separated.
Biodegradabe waste should be made compost and non-biodegradable waste
should be subjected to recycling and reusing. Necessary training for this
should be given to public.

6. Use of colour and dyes in food materials should be prohibited.

7. Toxic gases from industries should be filtered before allowing it to mix with
air.

8. Industries should be established far from human settlement.
Solid waste management

Any unwanted, useless materials generated in solid state as a result of various human
activities are known as solid waste. With the increase in population, solid wastes are
increasing day by day. It has now become a challenge to manage solid waste in a
proper way. A research shows maximum of solid wastes are organic, so if segregated
properly, the problem of solid waste can be solved easily.

In the past, solid wastes were managed locally. Almost all the wastes were used as
organic manure. But in the course of time, large volumes of solid wastes were
produced and the nature of solid wastes became very different. Due to which, it was
impossible to manage solid wastes locally. Till this date, it has been challenging to
manage solid wastes of large cities and towns even though government and a lot of
non-government organizations are being involved. Here are some of the significant
actions that can be taken in management of solid wastes.

 

169


Reduction in generation of solid wastes

The best and foremost action that should be taken in solid waste management
is to reduce its generation or production. This can be done by producing
quality products. Quality products have long life span so they run for a long
time, thus reduces the production of solid waste. Also, purchasing the product
only in absolute necessity also reduces the generation of solid waste.

Reuse of solid waste materials

Reuse or recycling of solid wastes is another important step in the management
of solid wastes. Some articles may have more than one application, such as, oil
and other food container can be used to store household good, plastic bags and
bottles can be recycled.

Generation of energy

The organic solid wastes can also be used for the generation of biogas. The
proper method should be applied for the generation of energy from solid waste
rich in organic matter.

Production of fertilizer from wastes

Solid wastes rich in organic matter can also be converted into compost manure.
They can also be used for vermiculture. These manures are rich in nitrogen,
phosphorus and potassium so are very good for agriculture.

Incineration

Incineration is the process of burning the wastes at high temperature, i.e. about
500-1200°C in a special type of fumace. The hot gases produced during
incineration can be used to operate turbine for the generation of electricity but
this method is not practiced in Nepal yet.

Land filling
This process refers to the disposal of solid waste materials by burying it at a
landfill site. Landfill silts are generally selected far from human residence.

Lots of considerations are taken both in engineering and environment level
before using certain land as landfill site.

1.

Cement is a fine grey powder of calcium carbonate (CaCO;) and special type
of clay (Al03SiO;) and is used in construction,

The raw materials that are used for the manufacture of cement are, lime stone,
special type of clay and gypsum.

Glass is hard, transparent, amorphous and super cooled liquid of metallic

Ceramics is special type of clay used for pottery, bricks kitchen wares, etc. It is

Polymers is an extremely large molecule consisting of a chain or network of
small molecules called monomers. The process of forming polymer by large
number of monomers is known as polymerization.

Plastics is a manmade polymer which can be moulded to any shape when they
re-warm.

The plastic which become soft on heating and hard on cooling is known as
thermoplastics.

The plastics which are hard and strong and are resistant to heat are known as
thermosetting plastics.

Soap is a salt of fatty acids that is capable of carrying away dirt along with
water.

Detergent is a synthetic organic compound obtained from hydrocarbon such as
alkyl benzene sulphonate

The fertilizers are the substances that are added to the soil to replenish the
nutrients consumed by growing plants.

Man made poisonous chemicals that are used for destroying or killing harmful
insects are known as insecticides.

Fibres are thread like strands which are strong materials used for making
clothes, nets, ropes, etc.

The pollution caused by excessive use and improper disposal of chemicals is
called chemical pollution.

Any unwanted, useless materials generated in solid state as a result of various
human activities are known as solid waste. Solid waste management can be
done by reduction in solid waste generation, reuse of solid waste, generation of
energy from solid waste, production of fertilizer from waste, incineration and
land filling.
