Unit 14 Invertebrates

You must have seen people involved in rearing silkworms and honey bees.
Why do you think people rear silkworms and honey bees? What types of
animals are these insects?

Silkworms and honey bees are the invertebrates. Invertebrates are the animals
which do not possess vertebral column. Invertebrates are divided into nine
phyla including arthropoda. The animals kept under the phylum arthropoda
have jointed legs and their bodies are covered with a thick chitinous cuticle
called exoskeleton. This Phylum consists of insects like silkworm and honey
bees which are very usefull to us. People get silk from silkworms and highly
nutritious honey from honey bees. Silk is used to make clothes. The clothes
made from silk are soft and durable. In this unit, we will study about the body
structure, life cycle and uses of silkworms and honey bees.

SILKWORM

The rearing of silkworm for the production of silk is called sericulture.
Mostly, two types of silkworms are reared in Nepal for producing silk. They
are

 

i, Seri Silkworm (Bombyx mori)
ii, Eri Silkworm (Adtacus ricini)

Seri- Silkworm feeds on mulberry leaves and Eri Silkworm feeds on castor
leaves. Silk produced by Seri- Silkworm is shiny, creamy and white fibre
while Silk produced by Eri-Silkworm is creamy, white but less shiny. It has
been found that maximum quantity of silk (about 95%) produced in the world
is mulberry silk or seri silk.

Structure: An adult Silkworm is 2.5 cm long and
creamy white in colour. Its body is divided into
head, thorax and abdomen. Its entire body is
covered by minute hairy scales. The head bears a
pair of compound eyes, a pair of antennae and
reduced mouth parts. Thorax bears three pairs of
legs and two pairs of wings. Fig.14.1

 

Mate silkworm Female silkworm

Its proboscis is highly reduced as it does not feed during adult stage. The body
size of female silkworm is larger than the male because they have to produce
a large number of eggs.

175


Life Cycle of Silkworm

A silkworm is unisexual i.e. sexes ADULT FEMALE DUET MALE:
are separate. Male silkworm dies
immediately after mating but
female silkworm lays eggs and
dies after 4-5 days. The life cycle
has four stages which completes
within about 45 days.

 

i Egg
ii, Larva
iii, Pupa
iv. Adult eae

 

The life cycle of silkworm is called metamorphic life cycle due to having four
stages quite different from each other.

Egg: More than 300 eggs are laid upon the leaves of mulberry by the female
silkworm after mating. The eggs are whitish or yellowish white and semi
round. Normally, the eggs are hatched into larva after 10 to 12 days at about
the temperature of 25°C.

In sericulture, the eggs are placed in a cold storage until the mulberry leaves
are not available. It is because the larva of silkworm feeds on mulberry leaves
only. When the mulberry leaves become available, the eggs are kept in a
special incubator at the temperature of about 18°C. Gradually, the temperature
is increased up to 25°C. At this temperature, the eggs are hatched into larvae.

Larva: The larva of silkworm is called caterpillar. It lasts for 25 to 32 days.
The newly hatched larva is called first instar larva. It moves around in a
special looping manner. It has a small head with chewing type of mouth part
(madibulate) , large and segmented thorax with three pairs of true joined legs,
elongated and segmented abdomen with five pairs of unjointed prolegs
(pseudo legs) and a dorsal horn on eighth segment.

The spiracles present on cither side of the abdomen help in gaseous exchange.
The caterpillar is voracious and grows quickly. It sheds its skin four times. At
the time of shedding skin, it does not move and eat at all. The developmental
stage of larva between two successive moultings is called instar larva.

The well grown fifth instar larva starts secreting a sticky fluid from the gland
present on the lateral side of the body. When this fluid comes in contact to the
air, it turns into a long thread called silk. The thread gets wrapped around the

176


body of larva forming a round case called cocoon. The larva changes into
pupa within this cocoon.

Pupa: Pupa is the inactive stage of a silkworm. It does not eat and move at
all. But development of pupa continues inside the cocoon. The prolegs
disappear and two pairs of wings develop on the thorax. Normally, the pupa
becomes adult in 12 to 14 days

In the commercial field, silkworms are reared for producing silk. For this, the
cocoons are treated with hot air or hot water to kill the pupa present inside it.
Heating destroys the adhesive substance of cocoon so that silk can be
extracted easily.

The silk thread obtained in this way is soft, elastic, shiny, strong, long,
durable and light as well. That’s why it is also called queen of the fibres.

Some cocoons are left safe to obtain adults for the continuation of next
generation.

Adult: The pupa develops inside the cocoon into the adult silkworm. It
emerges out by breaking the cocoon. The adult silkworm has 3 pairs of legs
and 2 pairs of wings. It lives for 5 to 6 days. An adult silkworm does not feed
at all. This stage is just for reproduction.

Properties of Silkworm

The clothes made of silk are:

Light and more comfortable.

ii. High resistance to deformation.
iii, Soft and shiny.
iv. Strongest natural fibre.

v. Good insulation properties (warm in winter and cool in summer).

4.

i, Good water absorption and dries quickly.

vii.

Can take any colour.
Uses of Silk
i. Silk is used to make clothes, insulation coil of telephone etc.

ii, The dead pupae obtained while extracting silk thread is highly
nutritious and can be used to feed livestock

HONEY BEES

Introduction: The honey bees are social insects which live in large colonies.
They secrete wax which solidifies on coming in contact with air. The wax is
used by them to form combs. The honcy collected by the honey bees is stored
in the comb. The combs are fixed on the support. The comb serves as a store
house for honey and the birth place of them. Honey producing bees belong to
bombydae, meliponidae and apidae. Different species of apidae are the main
honey producing bees. They are Apis mellifera, Apis florae, Apis dorsata,
Apis indica and Apis adamsoni. The honey bees are trimorphic as they are
found in three different forms in a colony namely queen, drones and workers.

 

Drone bee Queen bee Worker bee
fig.14.3 ‘fig.14.4 ‘fig.14.5
The Queen

The queen is the largest among all honey bees in a colony. It has shorter
wings and elongated and pointed abdomen. The duty of queen bee is to lay
egg and to coordinate all the worker bees and drone bees according to the
necessity of their colony. It produces a typical fragrance from its body which
is known by all the bees of the colony and easily find their way to come back
home during the collection of pollen grains and nectar.

The Drones

The drones are specially for fertilizing female queen and to keep their hive
warm. The individual drone that mates with queen immediately dies after
mating. They are blackish and more hairy, larger than worker bees but smaller

178


than the queen bees. They lack poisonous gland and sacks (pollen basket) to
collect pollen and nectar. They are fed by worker bees.

Workers

Worker bees are the smallest in size. The worker bees work on collecting
nectar and pollen visiting 20-30 flowers in a minute.

They have chewing and lapping type of mouth parts and a poisonous sting at
the end of abdomen. They have pollen basket at their hairy legs. Making
combs, collecting pollens and nectar to make honey, protecting the hives from
enemies, looking after eggs, larvae and pupae etc are the duty of worker bees.

The life cycle of the honey bees completes into four stages:

i Egg
ii, Larva
iii, Pupa
iv. Adult

Eggs: The queen lays about 1500-3000 eggs in a day. The queen chooses
standard worker size cell to release fertilized eggs where worker bees
develop. It chooses wider drone sized cells for developing drones where she
releases unfertilized eggs. The position of cach egg laid in the cell is upright
for the first day; it inclines the next day and lies horizontally on the third day.

 

Life cycle of bee

fig.14.6

179


Larvae: Afier 3-days, eggs are hatched into larvae. The larvae are voracious.
They feed on royal jelly for the first three days but later on they feed on honey
and pollen. It is seen that the larva that is chosen for future queen is given
royal jelly only as food. The larvae shed their skin five times. When they
become fully grown, they are sealed with wax in the cell by the worker bees.

Pupae: A great transformation takes place in this stage. Gradually, it starts
producing eyes, legs and hairs that cover their body. After 7- 14 days, pupae
chew up the wax and come out as adults.

Adults: Workers take 12 days, drones take about 14.5 days and queen takes
about 7.5 days to emerge from pupae. They feed on honey. Similarly, the
drones survive a few weeks to 4 months, workers survive 6-7 weeks and
queen survives about 2-5 years. The workers possess proboscis to extract
nectar. The duration of egg, larva, pupa and adult of all three forms of bees
can be shown in the following table

      

 

 

 

 

Bee Egg | Larva | Pupa Adult Total
Queen | 3days | 5.5 7.Sdays | 2-5 years 2-5 years
days
Drone | 3days | 6days |12days | Upto4month | Upto4
months
Worker | 3days | 6days | 14.5 days | 6-7 weeks Upto7
weeks

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

Division of labour according to age of worker bee.

* 1 to 3 days: They are also called infant bees. They are very weak
and fecd on scattered food, they learn to walk and help in cleaning the
comb. They also provide warmer to the comb.

© 4 to 6 days: They feed older larva and themselves with plenty of
honey and pollen.

© 7 to 11 days: Royal gland is developed in their head from where
royal-jelly is produced. The worker bees feed the royal jelly to the
young larva and also feed themselves with plenty of honey and
pollen.

© 12 to 17 days: The royal gland degenerate but 4 pairs of wax- gland
develop and secrete wax. Using the wax, worker bees of this age make

180


new combs and seal the cells with honey and that having filly grown
larvae,

© 18 to 20 days: Wax- glands degenerate but poison gland and sting is
developed. They guard their comb from enemies.

* 21 days: Now they fly out the comb to collect nectar, water, etc
from jungle, gardens and farm lands till their death.

When number of bees in the colony increases beyond its capacity, the worker
bees make new queen bee by feeding only royal- jelly to a selected larva
hatched from the fertilized egg. When new queen bee is made ready a new
colony is formed at the new place.

It is seen that the queen bee flics either only for mating (which is called
mating flight or nuptial flight) or for migration. Otherwise it remains inside
the comb laying eggs and eating honey.

Honey production: The nectar is collected by the worker bees and stores it in
their combs. The nectar then undergoes chemical change to form honey. The
maximum part of sucrose present in nectar gets converted into fructose and
glucose. Hence, the honey contains fructose, glucose, water, amino Acids etc
The honey is the source of vitamins, protein and minerals.

Uses of honey bees

1. Honey produced by honey bees is used as food. Honey is taken in the
form of sweet jelly or in the diluted form with bread, fruits, cereals etc. It
is used to prepare jam, honey cakes, breads, biscuits etc.

2. It is used as a medicine to remove weakness, mental disorder, fatigue,
nervous disorder. It also acts as natural bactericide, removes gastric ulcer,
provides relief from hyper acidity

Bee wax is used to make creams, lotions, face packs etc.

4, During the collection of nectar and pollen, worker bees help in
pollination in flowers by which they help in increasing crop production.

GLOSSARY
Probosis: an extensible tubular sucking organ
Hive: a place where bees are kept

Metamorphosis: a series of changes in the organisms from egg
to the adult form

Moulting: periodic shedding of the skin

Royal jelly: a highly nutritious food of the queen

Voracious: to eat greedily

Colony: an aggregate of worker bees, drones and a queen living
together in a hive as one social unit

Trimorphic: occurring or having in three different forms

Dioecious: unisexual

Comb: a waxy structure made by honey bees having
numerous hexagonal cells to lay eggs and to store honey
and pollen

Human Nervous and
Glandular Systems

Unit 15.

How do you feel when you suddenly touch a hot object? What makes you feel
hot? Which body systems, do you think, are involved in the process of
reaction to heat?

All these things happen in our body due to the presence of sense organs,
nervous system and glandular system. When we touch any hot object, we
remove our hand quickly. It happens because our body responds to the
external stimulus, heat. This response of the body is involuntary. It occurs due
to an integrated coordination among sense organs, nervous system and other
parts of the body. Our brain is the master controller of our body.

 

Similarly, various body activities are coordinated, integrated and regulated by
the hormones produced by endocrine glands. These hormones regulate body
growth, mental development, sexuality and other metabolic activities. In this
chapter, we are going to discuss on the human nervous system and the
glandular system.

NERVOUS SYSTEM

Nervous system is one of the major systems of our body which controls and
coordinates all the body activities. It is made up of billions of nerve cells
called neurons. A neuron is capable of initiating nerve impulse in response to
stimulus and transmitting it. It has made us capable to react to the external
environment. The nervous system gives information of the external
environment through the sense organs. It also regulates the functions of
voluntary and involuntary muscles in a balanced way.

The entire nervous system is divided into 3- parts.
1. Central Nervous System(CNS)

2. Peripheral Nervous System(PNS)

3. Autonomic nervous System(ANS)

Central Nervous System (CNS):

It lies along the mid-dorsal part of the body and consists of brain and spinal
cord.

Brain: Brain is the largest and uppermost part of Central Nervous System. It
is enclosed within the bony structure called cranium. It is covered or protected

185


by three membranes called meninges.
They are duramater, piamater and
arachnoid mater. Duramater is the
outermost layer and piamater is the
innermost layer of the meninges. In
between arachnoid mater and piamater
there is a fluid called cerebrospinal fluid
which protects the brain from injury.

Brain is divided into 3 main parts:

Cerebrum, Cerebellum and Medula oblongata.

 

1.

Cerebrum: It is the largest part of the brain which occupies about 80%
of the whole brain. It extends in frontal, parietal and occipital region. It is
divided into two hemispheres; left and right. Both the hemispheres are
separated by a deep longitudinal fissure. The outer surface of the
cerebrum has convolutions, which increase its surface area. The outer
surface consists of grey matter whereas inner surface consists of white
matter. It controls various activities such as smell, speech, memory,
hearing, touch, excitement, anger, interpretation, etc. It also controls
other parts of the brain. Injury in this part may lead a person towards
coma,

Cerebellum: It is the second largest part of the brain present below the
posterior portion of the cerebrum. It consists of two cerebellar
hemispheres with a central worm shaped part, vermis. It has grey matter
outside and white matter inside. Injury in this part leads to the failure of
voluntary muscles and causes paralysis.

Functions

Dy)
2)

3.

Cerebellum balances the body.

It controls rapid muscular activities like running, typing, talking, etc.

 

Do you know?

Alcohol affects cerebellum and it disturbs muscular activities.

 

 

Medulla oblongata: It is the lowermost part of our brain. It lies just
above the spinal cord. Like cerebrum and cerebellum, it consists of grey
matter and white matter. Injury of this part causes instant death.

 

186

 


Functions:

i. Medulla oblongata receives and integrates signals from spinal cord and
sends resulting signals to the cerebellum.

ii, It controls involuntary functions like vomiting, coughing, sneezing,
swallowing food, breathing, enzyme and hormone secretion, blood
pressure, salivation, etc.

iii. It promotes peristalsis of alimentary canal and dilation of blood vessels.

Spinal cord: Spinal cord is the elongated part
of central nervous system which extends from
the atlas to the first lumbar vertebrae. Its
outer part consists of white matter and inner
part consists of grey matter. It is protected by
a bony structure called vertebral column. The
white matter in outer part gives shiny
appearance to the spinal cord. It is also
protected by meninges and cerebrospinal
fluid. Any damage or injury to the spinal cord
causes paralysis of the lower parts.

Functions

 

1) Spinal cord conducts reflex actions.

2) It connects various body parts with the brain.

Peripheral nervous system

The Peripheral nervous system consist 12 pairs of cranial nerves and 31 pairs
of spinal nerves. They connect central nervous system with the organs and
muscles. The cranial nerves arise from the brain and spinal nerves arise from
the spinal cord. Cranial nerves are sensory, motor and mixed type. They
communicate eye, nose, tongue, ear etc. to the brain. Spinal nerve tissues are
responsible for conducting impulse between various parts of the body and
brain, They are mixed type of nerves.

Autonomic nervous system

Autonomic nervous system is an involuntary part of the nervous system. It
controls the involuntary activities of the various organs like secretion of
enzymes for food digestion. The secretion of enzymes is autonomic function.
This nervous system is divided into two parts.

1) Sympathetic nervous system

187


2) Parasympathetic nervous system

Sympathetic and parasympathetic nervous system are opposite in nature.
Sympathetic nervous system increases blood pressure; heart beat rate at the
time of sudden incidents and parasympathetic nervous system normalizes the
situations.

Activity 15.1

Prepare a model of central nervous system by using clay soil. Colour it.
Display it in the classroom.

Neuron: A neuron or a nerve cell is the structural and functional unit of
nervous system, Human nervous system has about 100 billion neurons. A
neuron consist single cell body (soma) which has 5 to 7 small branches called
dendrites and a long branch called axon like a typical cell, the cell body
(soma) consists nucleus, cytoplasm and cell membrane (neuroplasm). Cyton is
concerned with metabolic maintenance and growth.

The dendrites are the short branches of a neuron which are responsible to
receive nerve impulse. They receive impulse and send towards the cell body.

Axons are myelinated or non myelinated. They conduct nerve impulse away
from the cell body and pass to the dendrites of next neuron. Myclinated axons
have periodic constriction and in these areas myelin sheath is absent. Such
areas are called nodes of ranvier. The axon ends in a group of branches called
the axon terminals.

On the basis of their functions, neurons are of three types:

1. Sensory neuron or afferent deville:
neuron: sensory neuron transmit

Synapses
The axon of a neuron branches into minute filaments which, terminates in
small swellings (synaptic knobs) which are very close to the dendrites and the
cell body of next neuron. Hence, the minute gap between synaptic knob of a
neuron and dendrites and cell body of a next neuron is called synapse.

Ganglion

The collection of cell bodies or cytons is called ganglion. It communicates
with the brain and spinal cord to regulate involuntary function.

Glandular system

When we see or hear any sour food material, salivation occurs. The saliva is
released from the salivary gland in our mouth. Likewise, tear gland (lacrimal
gland) secretes tear, when dust enters into the eyes.

There are many tissues and organs in the body which secretes such fluids

mostly in the form of hormones and enzymes. This group of tissues and
organs which secrete enzymes and hormones is called glandular system.

There are two types of glands classified on the basis of presence or absence of
duets,

1) Exocrine glands
2) Endocrine glands

1) Exocrine glands: These are the ducted glands which secrete enzymes
for various metabolic activities. Their secretions are carried to the target
organs through the duct. Examples: salivary glands, pancreas, etc.

2) Endocrine glands: These are the ductless glands which secretes
hormones. They are responsible for controlling and regulating various
body activities. Their secretions are directly passed into the blood stream
to reach the specific organ.

A hormone is a chemical substance which control, coordinate and
integrates various physiological processes and activities of the body. So,
hormones are called chemical messengers

Fig.: 15.4 endocrine glands

Pituitary gland (Master gland): The pituitary gland is located below the

hypothalamus of the brain. It is small pea sized gland attached to the brain by

a stalk, the infundibulum. It has three lobes namely anterior, intermediate and
posterior.

Pituitary gland secretes two types of hormones:
i. Tropic hormone or stimulating hormone
ii, Non tropic hormone or growth hormone

The tropic hormone is also called stimulating hormone which stimulates other
glands for their secretions. For example, Thyroid stimulating hormone (TSH)
or thyrotrophic which controls the activity of thyroid gland. Other examples
are: follicle stimulating hormone (FSH), luteinizing hormone (LH), prolactin
hormone, etc.

The growth hormone (GH) or somatotrophin stimulates growth of the body.
Hyper secretion of growth hormone causes gigantism during childhood and in

190


adults. It causes acromegaly (enlargement of the bones of hand, feet and face).
Hypo- secretion of GH causes dwarfism in the children.

Thyroid gland: Thyroid gland is a largest gland and is bilobed and located on
the either side of trachea, The hormones secreted by thyroid glands are
thyroxin, tri-iodothyroxin, thyrocalcitonin (TCT).

The thyroid hormones are responsible for regulating the metabolic rate of the
body, stimulate protein synthesis, maintain body temperature, and suppress
the release of calcium ions from the bones and accelerate mental
development. Thyroxin is rich in iodine. It helps in normal growth and
development of the body.

Hyper secretion of thyroid hormone causes exophthalmic goiter which is
characterized by protrusion of the eye balls, weight loss, increased body
temperature, increased heart beat rate, restlessness, increased appetite, etc.

Hypothyroidism i.e. less secretion of thyroid hormone causes cretinism in the
children characterized by physical and mental retardation, potbelly, slow
heartbeat, lower blood pressure, etc. It may also cause myxoedema or gull’s
disease in the adult. It is characterized by the dry skin, the lack of alertness,
intelligence and initiative, low blood pressure, retarded sexual development.
Similarly it may cause simple goiter mostly in the people of hilly areas. It is
characterized by the thyroid enlargement. It occurs due to the deficiency of
iodine in food.

Parathyroid glands: These glands are four in number and are located on the
posterior surface of thyroid glands. The hormone secreted by this gland is
Parathyroid hormone or parathormone. This hormone regulates the amount of
calcium and phosphate in the blood.

Hyperparathyroidism stimulates the release of more calcium from the bones.
It results softening of bones. Also bones become porous and weak. This
disorder is called osteoporosis. They easily get fractured. It may also cause
the deposition of excess calcium in the soft parts of the body like kidneys in
the form of stone.

Similarly, Hypothyroidism causes significant decrease in the calcium level,
which causes the convulsions and cramps of muscles. This disorder is
hypocalcaemic tetany or parathyroid tetany.

Adrenal Glands: These glands are two in number each located at the top of
each kidney. Each adrenal gland has outer adrenal cortex and inner adrenal
medulla. The hormones produced by this gland are adrenaline and nor-
adrenaline and cortisone. They prepare the body for various functions. Nor-

191


adrenaline maintains or regulates blood pressure under normal conditions.
Adrenaline prepares the body daring to face the emergency situations. It
increases heart beat rate at the time of emergency. Cortisone regulates the
metabolism of fat, protein and carbohydrate to address stress. The hyper
secretion of adrenaline increases the high blood pressure, heart beat rate but
hypo secretion of adrenaline causes weakness, lower blood pressure, nausea,
vomiting, unconsciousness etc.

Pancreas: Pancreas is present in the abdominal region and has both endocrine
and exocrine functions, so, it is also called heterocrine gland or mixed gland.
The endocrine part consists of islets of langerhans, which secrete insulin and
glucagon hormones.

By the combined effect of insulin and glucagon blood sugar level is
maintained, Glucagon stimulates the liver to change glycogen into glucose.
But insulin reduces the blood sugar level by converting glucose into glycogen
and helps body cells to consume glucose to liberate energy. So, insulin and
glucagon are just opposite in function.

Hyper secretion of insulin causes hypoglycemia characterized by weakness,
profuse sweating, confusion, unconsciousness, convulsions and hunger.

Hypo secretion of insulin causes diabetes mellitus (Hyperglycemia)
characterized by more glucose level in the blood than the normal range,
excessive urination, dehydration, intense(excessive) thirst, low healing power
etc.

Gonads: Gonads are the sex glands called ovaries and testes in female and
male, respectively. The chief hormones secreted by ovaries are oestrogen and
progesterone, Oestrogen stimulates the development of secondary sexual
characteristics in female like enlargement of breast, growth of pubic hair etc.
and progesterone prepares the female body for pregnancy. Testosterone is the
male hormone. Testosterone stimulates the development of secondary sexual
characteristics in males like facial hair (beard, moustache), hoarse voice,
pubic hair, etc.

Summary

1.

The entire nervous system is divided into 3 parts. Central Nervous
System (CNS), Peripheral Nervous System (PNS) and Autonomic
nervous System (ANS).

Brain is the largest and uppermost part of the Central Nervous
System.

A neuron or a nerve cell is the structural and functional unit of the
nervous system.

A neuron consists of single cell body (soma) which has 5 to 7 small
branches called dendrites and a long branch called axon.

The minute gap between synaptic knob of a neuron and dendrites and
cell body of a next neuron is called synapse.

The Peripheral nervous system consists 12 pairs of cranial nerves and
31 pairs of spinal nerves.

‘Autonomic nervous system is an involuntary part of the nervous
system.

There are two types of glands classified on the basis of presence or
absence of ducts, exocrine glands and endocrine glands.

Pituitary gland is also called a master gland as it controls and regulates
the secretion of other glands.

Thyroid gland secretes thyroxine hormone which regulates
metabolism in the body

Parathyroid gland secretes parathormone which balances the calcium
and phosphate salt between bone and blood.

Pancreas is called mixed gland because it produces both engymes and
hormones.

Adrenal secretes adrenaline which prepares a person to face
emergencies.

Gonads secrete sex hormones which develop secondary sexual
characteristics in males and females.

Unit 16 Blood Circulation in
Human Body

What will happen if oxygen, nutrients, and hormones do not reach our body
cells? Which body system is involved in the process of supplying these
substances? What, do you think, will happen to life if there is not sufficient
supply of these substances to the body cells?

Actually, all the nutrients, gases and hormones are transported to the entire
body cells through blood. All these things are life-supportive. Blood is the
main component of circulatory system of our body. The circulatory system
also returns excretory materials produced in the body to the excretory parts.
This system consists of blood, blood vessels and heart.

Blood Circulatory System

RBCs WI Platelets
(Erythrocytes) (Leucocytes)—(Thrombocytes)

Blood flows in the blood vessels and carries necessary gases, nutrients,
hormones etc. and is continuously pumped by heart. This type of blood
circulation is called closed circulatory system. Arteries distribute pure
(oxygenated) blood to various parts of the body and veins return
deoxygenated blood to the heart.

196


Structure of blood
Red blood col

Blood is the fluid connective tissue that contains
plasma and cells or blood corpuscles. It is
slightly alkaline fluid with pH 7.4. It supplies all
the necessary materials required for the life
Blood contains blood plasma (55%) and blood
cells (45%). A healthy adult person has about 5.5
litre blood.

White blood cat
Plasma

Pistatets

 

Fig.: 16.1 structure of blood
Blood plasma

It is the yellowish substance that contains 90% water and 10% organic and
inorganic substances. The organic substances present in plasma are protein,
carbohydrate, fat etc. and inorganic matters like salts.

Function of plasma
i. Plasma regulates the amount of water in the blood.

ii, It transports absorbed food materials and oxygen into various parts of the
body.

iii, It carries urea from liver to the kidneys for removal.
iv. It transports hormones secreted by endocrine glands.

v. The proteins present in the plasma are albumin, globulin and _ fibrinogen.
Fibrinogen helps in clotting of blood.

vi. Plasma contains anti coagulant substance called heparin which prevents
blood clotting inside the blood vessels.

Blood cells or blood corpuscles:

There are three types of blood cells. Blood cells remain floating in the blood

plasma. The three types of blood cells are:

a) Erythrocytes or RBCs

b) Leucocytes or WBCs

c) Thrombocytes or platelets

a) Erythrocytes or RBCs: Erythrocytes are non-nucleated, biconcave _ or
circular blood cells which transport gases (O2 and CO:) in the blood. The
number of RBCs per cubic mm of blood is about 45 lakh to 50 lakh.
Their life span is about 90 to 120 days. It has been found that about 20

lakh RBCs are being destroyed in the liver and spleen and are formed in
the bone marrow in every second.

197


b)

A ted pigment called haemoglobin is found in the red blood cells. It
contains iron. It absorbs sufficient oxygen from lungs and transports
throughout the body in the form of oxyhaemoglobin.

The absence of adequate number of RBCs in the blood causes anaemia.
In such condition, a person feels tired even after a short walk.

Leucocytes or WBCs: WBCs are large sized nucleated blood cells.
They are formed in the bone marrow, lymph nodes, spleen. Their
number is 4000-10000 per cubic mm of circulating blood. WBCs are
round or irregular shaped and can change their shape like amoeba. This
changeable body shape enables them to squceze out from the wall of
capillaries during the attack of germs. WBCs are colourless. The life span
of WBC is about two weeks

There are two types of leucocytes:
i) Granular leucocytes
ii) Agranular leucocytes

Granular leucocytes: Granular leucocytes contain granules in their
cytoplasm. They are of three different types: Eosinophils, Basophils and
neutrophils. Eosinophils are non-phagocytic. The main function of
eosinophil is to destroy toxins. Basophils are least in number among the
leucocytes. They release heparin, serotonin, etc. They are mostly amoeba
like and ingest small particles like carbon. Neutrophill are the largest in
number among all the leucocytes. They engulf germs and are called
phagocytes

Agranular leucocytes: Agranular leucocytes lack granules in their
cytoplasm. There are two types of agranular leucocytes. i.e. lymphocytes
and monocytes. Lymphocytes produce antibodies to destroy germs.
They also help in healing injury. They are smaller in size. Monocytes are
the largest leucocytes. They are motile and phagocytic in nature which
engulf bacteria.

c) Thrombocytes or platelets

Platelets are non-nucleated and oval shaped blood cells. These are
formed from the large cells of bone marrow. The life span platelets is
about 2 to 3 days. Their number in per cubic mm of the blood is 2 lakh to
4 lakh.

198


Platelets are responsible for blood clotting at the site of injury or cut. At
the site of injury or cut, they produce a chemical called thromboplastin
which helps in the clotting of blood.

Excess of platelets in the blood is called thrombocytosis and decrease in
the number of platelets causes thrombocytopenia. Platelets are the
fragments of the cells rather than true cells.

Functions of blood

 

Transportation: Blood supplies oxygen from lungs to body cells
and carbon dioxide from the cells to the lungs for removal. The
absorbed nutrients are transported to the body cells and tissues. The
hormones produced by various endocrine glands are transported to
target parts. Likewise, it carries unwanted materials to the liver,
lungs, kidney for their removal.

Regulation: Blood regulates the body temperature. It regulates the
amount of fluids and various chemicals in the tissues.

Protection: Blood destroys toxic substances and disease causing
germs. It maintains immunity power of the body. Fibrinogen along
with thromboplastin present in the blood helps in blood clotting at
the site of cut or injury.

Blood Vessels

 

Mainly there are three types of blood vessels. Endothelium smooth musclt ©"

They are arteries, veins, and capillaries. Blood
flows from the heart to various body parts
through arteries. The arteries divide and
redivide to form capillaries which supply blood
to various body cells. The capillaries unite to

 

199

  


form venules and ultimately the vennules unite to form veins. Veins return
deoxygenated blood the heart.

Arteries: Arteries are thick walled blood vessels that usually supply
oxygenated blood from the heart to the different parts of the body. Thicker
muscular wall of arteries resist higher blood pressure. The wall of the arteries
is three layered: outermost connective tissue, middle smooth muscle and
innermost endothelium.

The arteries are formed by the branching of aorta. The aorta divides into
arteries which further divide to form arterioles. The arterioles are divided into
a network of fine vessels called capillaries. Any damage or injury in the
arteries may cause death of a person.

Veins: Veins are thin walled blood vessels that return deoxygenated blood
from various body parts to the right auricle of the heart. Blood pressure is low
in the veins. They have valves which help to pass the blood in the forward
direction. The wall of veins is also formed of three layers as that of the
arteries.

Capillaries: Capillaries are the fine network of the blood vessels which
connect arteries and veins. Their wall is formed by a single layer of
endothelial cells. This extra thin wall of capillaries helps them to exchange
gases, nutrients and waste materials between the cells and the blood.

  
 

Heart

The heart is a muscular organ which

pumps blood throughout the body. It is Pulmonary artery
made of cardiac muscles. It lies in the tetsu
thoracic cavity and is enclosed within a Right atrium a Mitral valve
pericardium, In between the two layers Of pi... vahe ~ ponte valve
pericardium pericardial fluid is present “Tycuspid vaive WY ~ Let enticue

which protects the heart from injury. It
makes the frictionless movement of the
heart. The heart has two upper chambers
called auricles or atrium and two lower chambers called ventricles. Auricles
are thin walled whereas ventricles are thick walled. Thicker wall of ventricles
provide sufficient force to pump the blood at the greater distance in the body.

 

Auricles are separated from each other by a vertical septum called interatrial
septum. The auricles are separated from ventricles by atrio- ventricular
septum. Similarly, ventricles are separated from each other by inter
ventricular septum. Left ventricle is thicker than right ventricle. The thicker
wall of left ventricle provides sufficient force to pump the blood throughout

200


the body. Right ventricle only pumps the blood to the lungs. Coronary arteries
present there to supply pure blood to the heart muscles.

There are four valves in the heart:

i. Tricuspid valve: Tricuspid valve is located between right auricle and
right ventricle. It has three muscular flaps. Blood passes from right
auricle to the right ventricle through this valve. When the right ventricle
contracts, tricuspid valve closes and blood passes towards lungs through
pulmonary artery.

ii. Bicuspid valve: Bicuspid valve lies between the left auricle and left
ventricle. It has two muscular flaps. Oxygenated blood comes from the
lungs through four pulmonary veins to the left auricle. When left auricle
contract ,the bicuspid valve open and blood from left auricle passes to
left ventricle.Similarly,when left ventricle contracts, bicuspid valve
closes and blood passes towards aorta.

iii, Pulmonic valve: Pulmonic valve is present at the opening of pulmonary
artery. The pulmonary artery arises from the right ventricle. When right
ventricle contracts, this valve opens and blood passes towards the lungs.
It is semilunar shaped.

iv. Aortic valve: Aortic valve is present at the opening of aorta. The aorta
arises from the left ventricle. When left ventricle contracts, aortic valve
opens and oxygenated blood passes to the whole body parts through
aorta.

Blood Circulation

The superior and inferior venacava pour deoxygenated blood to the right
auricle, Superior venacava returns deoxygenated blood from head, neck and
upper limbs to the right auricle whereas inferior venacava returns
deoxygenated blood from the lower parts of the body.

The deoxygenated blood from right auricle passes to the right ventricle
through tricuspid valve. Later, the right ventricle contracts and blood passes to

201


the lungs through pulmonary arteries for purification. The purified
(oxygenated) blood returns back to the left auricle through pulmonary veins.
When the left auricle contracts, blood passes to the left ventricle through
bicuspid valve. On the contraction of the left ventricle aortic valve opens and
oxygenated blood passes to the entire body through aorta.

Pulmonary Circulation (Heart lung circulation) and Systematic
Circulation (Body Circulation)

 

Fig.: 16.4 blood circulation

Circulation of blood between heart and lungs through pulmonary arteries and
pulmonary veins is called pulmonary circulation. It occurs to purify the
deoxygenated blood in the lungs.

Systemic circulation is the circulation of blood between the heart and different
body systems through arteries and veins. It occurs in the body to supply
oxygenated blood to the whole body through aorta. The deoxygenated blood
produced in the various body tissues and organs returns back to the heart
through veins.

Heart beat: The rhythmic contraction and relaxation of the heart is called
heart beat. Each heart beat has one contraction phase (systolic phase) and
relaxation phase (diastolic phase). Human heart beats about 70 — 80 times per
minute i.e. in average 72 times. Heart beats faster during sickness, exercise,
fear, excitement etc.

202


Blood Pressure: The force exerted by the flowing blood on the wall of blood
vessels is called blood pressure. In a normal person, blood pressure is about
120/80 mm of Hg.

When the ventricles contract blood pressure in the arteries increases and this
maximum blood pressure is called systolic blood pressure. But when
ventricles relax, blood pressure in the arteries decreases. This minimum blood
pressure observed in the arteries during the relaxation of ventricles is called
diastolic blood pressure. In a healthy person, systolic blood pressure is 120
mm of Hg and diastolic blood pressure is 80 mm of Hg. Blood pressure is
measured by an instrument called sphygmomanometer.

High Blood Pressure (Hypertension)

The blood pressure of 120/80 mm of Hg is considered as a normal blood
pressure. Sometimes it increases and its level reaches beyond 140mm of Hg
(systolic) and 90 mm of Hg (diastolic). It is considered high blood pressure.
High blood pressure is very dangerous as it may damage heart, brain, kidneys
ete.

Causes

i. A diet full of oily products
ii, Tobacco smoking

iii, Mental tension

iv. Kidney disorder

v. Lack of physical exercise
vi. Salty food

vii. Alcohol

viii. Age above 40 years

ix, Adrenal and thyroid disorder
x. Genetic reasons
Symptoms

1) Headache

2) Dizziness

3) Blurred vision

4) Chest pain

203


5) Short breathes
Preventive measures

Since high blood pressure is a dangerous body condition, one should be
highly conscious in the daily activities. Balanced dict helps to maintain
normal blood pressure. Oily and spicy foods should be avoided. Positive
thinking, disciplined behavior, regular physical exercise and regular health
check up help to balance normal blood pressure.

Diabetes

Diabetes is caused due to the deficiency of insulin in the body. Insulin deficit
causes the increased glucose level in the blood. So, this disease is also called
the disease of sugar. Insulin secreted by pancreas controls blood sugar level.

Causes of diabetes

1) Insufficient insulin production

2) Genetic reason

3) Stressful life style ete.

Symptoms

- Intense thirst

- Increased appetite

- Dizziness

- Unconsciousness

- Vision disorder

- Delay of wound healing ete.
Preventive measures

- Stress free daily activities

- Consumption of balanced diet

- Reducing body weight

- Stop taking alcohol, drugs, smoking
- Consuming green vegetables and fruits
- Regular physical exercise

- Having Sufficient water

204


Uric Acid

Uric acid is a chemical created when the body breaks down purines. Purines
are found in meat produets, pulses, beer etc. The breakdown of purines in the
foods that we cat results in the release of uric acid as a biproduct. Most uric
acid dissolves in blood and passes to the kidneys. If the body produces too
much uric acid and cannot be removed from the body, a person suffers from
uric acid. The high level of uric acid in the blood is called hyperuricemia.

Hyperuricemia is a condition of an excessive amount of uric acid in the blood
stream. Normally, uric acid is transported through blood to the kidneys and is
climinated in the urine. Accumulation of uric acid in the blood is either due to
failure to excrete uric acid or increased production of uric acid.

Causes

i. Hypothyroidism, obesity, leukaemia

ii. High level of caffeine and alcohol consumption.

iii, Excessive consumption of meat products, pulses, beers ete.
Symptoms

i, Development of gout- It happens due to the accumulation of uric
acid in the joint. It causes pain at the joints even in the minimal pressure.

ii, Kidney stone and kidney failure
iii, Hypertension

iv. Heart problem

Preventive measures

i, Reducing the intake of purine containing foods like meat and meat
products, sea food, legumes, ete.

ii, Frequently having strawberries and drinking black cherry juice.

iii. Taking sufficient water and have regular physical exercise.

1) The circulatory system is the main transport system that supplies oxygen,
nutrients, hormones, etc. to the different body cells and returns excretory
materials to the excretory parts.

2) Blood is the fluid connective tissue that contains plasma and cells or
blood corpuscles,

A red pigment called haemoglobin is found in the red blood cells.

capillaries.

The heart has two upper chambers called auricles and two lower
chambers called ventricles.

Circulation of blood between heart and lungs through pulmonary arteries
and pulmonary veins is called pulmonary circulation.

Systemic Circulation is the circulation of blood between the heart and

The rhythmic contraction and relaxation of the heart is called heart beat.

The force exerted by the flowing blood on the wall of blood vessels is
called blood pressure, In a normal person, blood pressure is about 120/80
mm of Hg.

Hyperuricemia is a condition of an excessive amount of uric acid in the
blood stream.

Chromosome and Sex
Determination

Unit 17

You must have noticed organisms producing off-springs of their own kind.
Which component, do you think, is responsible to make this possible, and to
determine the sex of animals? Discuss in groups.

 

Every organism produces off-springs of its own kind. Human beings
reproduce human beings: cats reproduce cats and so on. This phenomenon
tells us that the off-springs resemble their parents. This occurs due to the
transmission of characters from one generation to the next generation through
chromosomes present in the gametes (sperm and ovum) of the parents.

A chromosome consists of tiny structures called genes which are the segments
of DNA molecule. They carry hereditary characters. Chromosomes not only
transfer the hereditary characters but also determine sex of an individual.
Change in the number of chromosomes causes various disorders in the body.

Nucleus of the body cell of an organism

209


iii.

Metacentric chromosome: In this type of chromosomes, centromere
remains at the centre of a chromosome. It divides the arms of the
chromosomes equally into two halves.

Sub-Metacentric chromosome: In this type of chromosomes,
centromere is near one side which divides the chromosomes into two
unequal halves.

Acrocentric chromosome: In this type of chromosomes, centromere lies
near one end. It forms a very small arm beyond centromere.

Telocentric chromosome: In this type of chromosomes, centromere lies
at the extreme end and hence there is a single arm.

Number of chromosomes in different organisms

Due to the difference in the number of chromosomes in different organisms,
the organisms possess varied morphological features. It is seen that the
number of chromosomes in somatic cells or vegetative cells is diploid (2n)
while the number of chromosomes in a reproductive cell or gamete is haploid
(n). In the higher organisms in which sexual reproduction takes place, diploid
zygote is formed by the fusion of two haploid gametes of opposite sex each
from father and mother. The diploid zygote divides by mitosis process
maintaining the diploid chromosomes in each body cell.

Importance of chromosomes
i. Chromosomes are the sites for genes which carry hereditary characters.
ii, They play an important role in cell division, cell growth and cell repair.

At the time of sperm formation, 50% of the total sperms produced contain X

After copulation, if X chromosome containing sperm (male gamete) fuses

chromosome containing female gamete, a baby boy is produced.
Chromosomal disorder

Chromosomes transfer hereditary characters from the parents to their off-
springs. A chromosome is formed of many genes. A gene is the functional
segment of DNA. Each gene possesses a specific hereditary character.

The disorder in the body caused due to change in the number of chromosomes.
is called chromosomal disorder.

Down’s Syndrome

Down’s Syndrome (47, XY, +21) or (47, XX, +21): Down's syndrome
was first studied by John Langdon Down. It is a developmental disorder
caused by an extra number of 21° autosome. So, it is also called trisomy
21.

People with Down's syndrome have a flat face, a small broad nose, a
large furrow tongue, and upward slanting eyes. That has an increased risk
of respiratory infection, leukaemia, heart diseases, etc.

Klinefelter’s Syndrome (47, XXY): Klinefelter is a genetic disorder
caused when a person gets two X- chromosomes and one Y
chromosome. Mostly, such individuals develop as males.

Klinefelter's Syndrome is characterized by longer arms, legs, more fat,
wider hips, learning disability, abnormally tall.

Turner’s Syndrome (45, XO): It was first described by Dr. Henry

turner. It is a syndrome caused due to incomplete X- chromosome.
Mostly, such individuals develop as females.

Turner’s syndrome is characterized by shorter body structure, less
developed ovaries, short neck, etc.

Asexual and Sexual
Reproduction

Unit 18(A)

You must have noticed organisms giving birth to their off-springs. What, do
you think, are there various modes of reproduction among them?

All the living beings reproduce off-springs of their own kind for the
continuity of their generation. This is known as reproduction. There are
several methods of reproduction in plants and animals. Some animals give
direct birth to their babies while other reproduce by laying eggs. Some
produce the new generation from the parts of their body while some others
reproduce with the formation of gametes.

Generally, there are two modes of reproduction.
i. Sexual reproduction ii, Asexual reproduction
Sexual Reproduction

Sexual reproduction is a type which occurs by the fusion of male gamete and
female gamete. Most of the higher animals possess either a male reproductive
part or a female reproductive part in an individual. But in some lower
organisms like hydra, earthworm etc. both male and female gametes are
produced in the same individual. Such animals are called hermaphrodite or
bisexual.

In higher plants, flower of some plants are bisexual whereas some plants have
unisexual flower.

Features of sexual reproduction
1, Sexual reproduction requires the involvement of each male and female.
2. The offsprings are genetically different from their parents.

3. It occurs by the formation of gametes and their fusion.

4. The offsprings produced attain maturity after a long time.

Asexual reproduction

Asexual reproduction is a type which occurs without the involvement of
gametes. It means that asexual reproduction takes place through body parts,
splitting of the body, formation of spores and buds etc. The organisms
produced in this way are genetically identical to each other and also identical
to the parent.

21s


Features of asexual reproduction

1.

2.
3.
4

Only one parent involves in this process.
New organisms produced are genetically identical to their parent.
New organisms get matured within a short time.

Asexual reproduction is assisted by mitosis cell division in higher
organisms and direct cell division in lower and microscopic organisms.

Asexual reproduction in plants

1.

Fission is a method of asexual reproduction in which a unicellular

organism divides into two or more new organisms. It occurs in diatoms,

chlamydomonas, protozoans, bacteria etc. on:

Advantages of vegetative propagation
i. Genetically identical organisms are produced by vegetative propagation.
ii. It is a cheaper, faster and easier method of reproduction.

iii, It is a good method of reproduction of the plants which do not produce
good quality seeds like banana, sugarcane, rose, potato etc.

iv. Itis very useful to preserve the plants which are rare and endangered.
Asexual reproduction in animals

In animals, asexual reproduction mainly occurs through fission, budding, and
regeneration,

Fission: Fission is a method of asexual reproduction in the unicellular animals
in which their body divides into two or more than two new cells. It is common
in amoeba, paramecium, euglena, etc. Their body divides into two parts which
is termed as binary fission whereas multiple fission occurs in plasmodium.
During unfavourable condition, a cyst or a protective layer is formed in the
plasmodium, The nucleus inside it divides into many nuclei. Cytoplasm
deposits around each nucleus. The cyst wall breaks during favourable
condition and tiny cells are released and grow independently. Hence,
plasmodium follows multiple fission.

Budding: In budding, a new organism develops from an outgrowth called
bud. A bud is formed on the parent body which detaches eventually from the
parent body and grows independently. It is common in hydra.

Regeneration: In the animals like starfish, tapeworm, planaria, hydra etc. the
body divides into the fragments and cach fragment develop into the complete
organism. This method of reproduction is called regeneration.

Sexual Reproduction in plant

Flower is the reproductive part of angiosperms. A complete flower consists of
calyx, corolla, androecium and gynoecium as the floral parts. A flower having
both androccium (male part) and gynoccium (female part) is called a bisexual
flower whereas a flower having only cither gynoecium or androccium is
called a unisexual flower.

Calyx is the outermost whorl of a flower. It is a collection of sepals. Calyx
protects the inner parts of the flower during bud stage and helps in
photosynthesis. Calyx may be polysepalous (with free sepals) or
gamosepalous (with fused sepals).

Corolla is a group of colourful petals to attract Stigma
animals/insects. Its main function is to help in
pollination. Like calyx, a corolla may be
polypetalous or gamopetalous.

    

Androecium is the third whorl of a flower and
consists of stamens which are the male pyc
reproductive parts. A stamen has a bilobed
anther and a stalk like filament. Anther
consists of pollen sacs filled with pollen
grains. A pollen grain contains male gametes.

Sepal

Fig.: 18(A}.10

Gynoecium or pistil is the innermost whorl which is the female part of the
flower. A pistil may consist of a number of carpels. Each carpel consist of

219


three parts: a basal swollen ovary, a stalk like style and a terminal receptive
part called stigma. The ovary contain ovules. Female gamete or egg cell is
formed inside the ovule.

Activity 18(A)3

Collect some mustard flowers, study the different parts using hand lens one by

Pollination: The transfer of pollen grains from anther to stigma is called
pollination. Pollination occurs in two different ways.

1. Self pollination
2. Cross pollination

In the bisexual plants like wheat, pea, cotton, etc. self-pollination takes place.
It is the transfer of pollen grains from the anther to the stigma of the same
flower or from one flower to another flower of the same plant. In the bisexual
plants, anther and stigma mature simultaneously.

In the plants like maize, coconut, oak etc. cross pollination takes place. It
occurs both in bisexual and unisexual flowers. It is the transfer of pollen
grains from the anther of a flower of one plant to the stigma of a flower of
another plant but of the same species with the help of agents like wind, water,
animals, etc.

Fertilization

The fusion of male gamete and female gamete to form zygote is called
fertilization. In plants fertilization takes place after pollination. When the
male gametophytes called pollen grains reach to stigma, the pollen grains
germinate into long tube like structures called pollen tubes. A pollen tube
grows towards ovule. The two male gametes formed in a pollen grain pass
through the pollen tube. The pollen tube enters into the ovule through
micropyle. After entering into the ovule, the pollen tube enters into the
embryo sac. In the embryo sac, there are found three antipodal cells, two
synergids, a female gamete or egg cell and a secondary nucleus (a secondary
nucleus is formed by the fusion of two haploid nuclei).

 

220


After the pollen tube enters into the embryo sac, the apex of the tube dissolves
and male gametes are released. One of the male gametes fuses with the egg
cell or female gamete to form zygote. The poliengisin aa ¢
second male gamete fuses with secondary Stigma ——%
nucleus to form endosperm. This fusion of \
haploid male gamete(n) and diploid secondary style ——l
nucleus (2n) to form endosperm a triploid
structure (3n) is called triple fusion. Later on
zygote develops into embryo and endosperm
develops into endospermic tissue which
supplies nutrients to the growing embryo.
Hence, double fertilization takes place in the — Microplyle
angiosperms. After fertilization —ovule \
develops into seed and ovary develops into

fruit. Other parts gradually dry up.

     
    
  

\. Pollen tube

|
H

  

Fig.: 18(A).9

Sexual reproduction in animals

Sexual reproduction takes place in the animals by the fusion of sperm (male
gamete) and ovum (female gamete). Zygote is formed by the fusion of
gametes, The gametes are haploid (n) while the zygote is diploid (2n).
Fertilization in animals is classified into two types.

i) Internal fertilization
ii) External fertilization

Internal fertilization: Internal fertilization is a type of fertilization which
takes place by the fusion of male and female gametes inside the body of
female organism. Mammals, reptiles, birds, insects etc. have this type of
fertilization. The male releases sperms inside the reproductive part of female
during mating and a male gamete and a female gamete fuse inside to form
zygote. Internal fertilization is more safer.

External fertilization: External fertilization is the fusion of male gamete
(sperm) and female gamete (ovum) outside the body of an organism.
Generally it takes place in water. It is common in fishes and frogs. It is highly
affected by the external environmental factors.

 

Do you know?

Gametes are haploid (n) and are formed by the meiosis process. But a zygote
is diploid (2n) formed by the fusion of male and female gametes.

 

 

 

221


Advantages of asexual reproduction

i) Asexual reproduction is a short and simple method of reproduction.
ii) Only one individual parent involves in this process.

iii) The off-springs produced by this method are genetically identical.
Advantages of sexual reproduction

i) The off springs produced by this method are genetically different
from each other or from the parent cell.

ii) The variation caused due to sexual reproduction helps in the organic
evolution.

Summary

1. All the living beings reproduce of their own kind for the continuity of
their race. This is known as reproduction.

2. Sexual reproduction is a type which occurs by the fusion of male
gamete and female gamete.

»

Asexual reproduction is a type of reproduction which occurs without the
involvement of gametes.

4, Advantages of vegetative propagation

propagation.

ii, Itis a cheaper, faster and easier method of reproduction.

iii. It is a good method of reproduction of the plants which do not
produce quality seeds like banana, sugarcane, rose, potato, etc.

iv. It is very useful to preserve the plants which are rare and endangered.

5. The fusion of male gamete and female gamete to form zygote is
called fertilization.

6. The transfer of pollen grains from anther to stigma is called pollination.
7. Fertilization in angiosperms is double fertilization.

8. The fusion between secondary nucleus (2n) and a male gamete (n) is
called triple fusion.

Artificial Vegetative

Unit 18(8) Propagation in Plants

You must have seen farmers growing fruits and flowers artificially with the
use of modern technique. What, you think, are the various ways of artificial
reproduction in plants? What benefits do the farmers gain through such
reproduction of plants?

Artificial vegetative propagation is a rapid multiplication process in plants.
This method helps farmers to produce large number of new plants from a
single parent plant in an economic way. For this purpose, various parts of
plants like stem, root, and leaves can be used. This method can be applied in
case of plants that bear fruits and flowers.

According to the techniques used, it is of different types. Some common
methods are following.

Layering: In this method, the lower branch of a plant is inserted into the soil.

The part inside the soil gives rise to the roots from nodes which is separated

from the parent plant and grows independently. It is common in rose, jasmine,

lemon, strawberry etc.

Types of layering
i, Simple layering: In this method old branches of
the plants are bent and inserted inside soil. After 2-3
months, the inserted part develops roots. By
the end of rainy season, the rooted part is separated
from the parent plant and transferred in the nursery stn /
bed. It is common in lemon, nibuwa etc. Fig.: 18(8)1 Simple layering

      

Compound layering: In this method, the
maximum part of an old branch is inserted into
the soil, leaving the apex in the air. New plant
grows from the different nodes which are then
separated from parent plant and transferred into
the nursery bed. It is common in apple, pear, wall
nut ete.

 

225


iii. Tip layering: In this method, only the tip
of the branch is inserted into the soil. The
tip develops root and shoot. After 3-4
months the plant is separated from the
parent plant. It is common in blackberry.

 

iv. Mound or stool layering: Mound or
stool layering is suitable for heavy
stemmed trees with tight branches. In
the spring, the stems of young tree
are cut about 15cm above the trunk.
As a result, a number of new buds
develop. They are covered with soil
for roots to develop. After one year
the new plants are ready to be
transferred to nursery beds. It is suitable si
for mango, guava, pear, plum etc. Fig.: 18(B)4 Mound or stool layering

 

Air layering: In this method, a piece of
branch is scrapped by removing a ring of
the bark. Rooting hormone is placed at the
cut parts. The cut part is covered with
grafting clay and wrapped with a
polythene sheet. After a month, roots
appear. The rooted part is separated from the
parent plant and is planted into the soil. It is
common in the plants like Mango.

 

Fig.: 18(B)5 Air layering

Grafting: It is a modern technique in which a part of one plant is transplanted
to other plant of closely related variety or species. The part of the plant with
the root system is stock and the part of the plant which is grafted is called
scion. It is common in apple, mango, peach etc.
Types of grafting Fon
i. Whip grafting: Whip grafting is suitable method
when the stock and scion are of similar diameter
and size. A slanted cut of about 3.5cm is made in
both the scion and stock. After the cuts are made, stock
both the parts are kept together tightly so as both
Fig.: 18(B)6 Whip grafting
226


the surfaces match closely are wrapped with plastic or rubber. After 2-3
months, when both parts join properly, the wrapper is removed.

ii. Tongue Grafting: it is also an easy and
reliable method of grafting. In this method,
the lower part of the scion and upper part of
the stock are cut to make 3cm to Sem long
slopes. A fissure of about 1cm depth is made.
The cut parts of scion and stock are fixed
together and it is made air tight by wrapping
with plastic. When both the parts scion and
stock join together, the plastic is removed. Fig.: 18(B)7 Tongue Grafting

fused strongly and the tape is removed. Fig.: 18(B}6 Whip grafting

Tissue culture: This is a modern technique of reproduction in which a cell or
tissue of a plant is grown in an artificial medium to regenerate new plant. In
this method, a cell or a part of tissue is cultured in an artificial culture medium
which consists nitrogen and hormones like auxin, cytokinin in a balanced
condition. The tissue grows into an unorganized mass called callus. The callus
is divided into many pieces and is kept in another culture medium where roots
and shoots grow and plantlets are formed. The plantlets are transferred into
the soil for their independent growth.

227


Advantages of tissue culture

1.

A large number of genetically identical organisms are produced by
this method.

 

2. It helps to preserve the rare and endangered plants by their rapid
multiplication.
Summary

1. Artificial vegetative propagation is a rapid multiplication process in
plants. For this purpose, various parts of plants like stem, root and leaves
can be used.

2. During layering, the lower branch of a plant is inserted into the soil. The
part inside the soil gives rise to the roots which is separated from the
parent plant and grows independently.

3. Grafting is a modern technique in which a branch of one plant is
transplanted to other plant of closely related variety or species. The part
of the plant with the root system is stock and the part of the plant is
grafted is called scion.

4. Tissue culture is a modern technique of reproduction in which a cell or
tissue of a plant is grown in an artificial medium to regenerate new plant
ina large number.

5. Artificial methods of vegetative propagation have great importance in
horticulture, forestry, agriculture as well as in many scientific researches.

Unit 19 Heredity

‘You must have noticed similarities and differences between children and their
parents in terms of their physique. For example, the colour of skin, the colour
of eyes, the shape of nose, etc. Parents and their children are similar but not
exactly identical. What, do you think, is the reason behind this?

Heredity is the transfer of characteristics from one generation to the next
generation. This inheritance of characters from one generation to the next
generation takes place in all the living beings. It occurs through the
chromosomes present in the gametes (sperm and ovum) of the parents. The
chromosomes present in the sperm and ovum carry all the parental
characteristics. Due to this all the organisms reproduce offsprings of their own
kind.

Chromosomes are the sites for the genes which are actually the hereditary
carriers. The offsprings resemble their parents on one hand and on the other
hand, they are seemed different from their parents.

It happens due to crossing over during gamete formation and also due to the
environmental factors, Gradual change occurs in the organisms during the
course of adaptation to the changing environment by the organisms.

In this unit, we study about the Mendel’s experiment, laws and variation.

Key Terms to Understand Heredity

Gene: A gene is a tiny segment of DNA which determines the particular
characteristic.

Alleles: An allele is one of the other forms of the same gene. Example: In Tt,
T is an allele of ‘t’ and vice versa.

Homozygous Organism (Pure Organism): An organism having two
identical alleles (genes) that determine a particular characteristic. Example:
Tall pea plant having TT.

Heterozygous Organism: An organism having two different alleles is called
heterozygous organism. For example, Hybrid tall pea plant with genotype Tt.

Phenotype: The external appearance or expression of an organism is called
phenotype.

Genotype: The genetic makeup of an organism of a particular character is
called genotype.

230


First filial generation: The offsprings produced by the cross between two
pure parents are called first filial generation or F, generation.

Second Filial Generation: The offsprings produced from the first generation
are called second filial generation or F generation.

Dominant and recessive characters: When a cross is made between two
pure individuals having or considering a pair of contrasting characters, only
one character appears externally in the first generation and another remains
hidden. The character which is expressed in the first generation is called
dominant character and the character which remains hidden in the first
generation is called recessive character.

Monohybrid and dihybridcross: When a cross is made between two pure
individuals having or considering a pair of contrasting characters, the cross is
called monohybrid cross. When a cross is made between two pure individuals
having or considering two pairs of contrasting characters, the cross is called
dihybrid cross.

 

Symbols given to the genes

English alphabets are used to symbolize the genes. The Alphabet used is the
first letter of the character. The dominant gene is symbolized with the capital
letter and recessive gene is symbolized with small letter. For example,T and t
are used to symbolize tall (Dominant) and dwarf (recessive) characters
respectively.

Mendel’s Experiment

Gregor John Mendel is known as the pioneer in the field of genetics. He did
his research work on common garden pea (Pisum sativum) to prove that the
hereditary characters transfer from parents to their offsprings and to know the
rules that they follow while transferring characters from parents to their
offsprings. Mendel selected pea plant for his research due to the following
reasons.

i. Pea plants have short life span and can be grown easily.

ii, Pea plants are bisexual. So, they are self pollinating plants. But
cross pollination can also be done when necessary.

iii, The hybrids produce fertile offsprings.
iv. Pea plants are available in the number of contrasting characters.
v. They are cultivated easily and are easily available,

vi. They produce a large number of offsprings.

231


Mendel selected seven pairs of the contrasting characters of the pea plant
for his research, The characters are given below:

i. Height of the plant: Tall(TT) and —dwarf(tt)
ii, Position of the flower: Axial(AA) and Terminal(aa)
iii, Colour of the flower: Red(RR) and White (rr)
iv. Colour of the pod : Green(GG) and __yellow(gg)
v. Shape of the pod : Inflated(I1) and —_constricted(ii)
vi. Shape of the seed : Round(RR) and wrinkled(rr)
vii. Colour of the seed : Yellow(YY) and green(yy)

Mendel conducted research on the pea plant using seven pairs of contrasting
characters as mentioned above. During the course of his experiment first he
confirmed the pure breeding plants by allowing them for self pollination.
Then, he conducted his experiment making a cross between two pure
individuals having a pair of contrasting characters.

He made a cross between pure tall pea plant and pure dwarf pea plant. All the
offsprings produced in this generation were tall and named as F; generation or
first filial generation. He conducted his experiment of the similar type using
other pairs of contrasting characters. He found the same result in all the cases.

  

Pure tall Hybrid tall Pure dwarf

9.1 Pure all, Hybrid tall and pure dwarf Pea plants

Fig

 

The character which was expressed externally in the F generation was named
as dominant character and the hidden character in the first generation was
named as the recessive character.

232


When the hybrid pea plants obtained in the first generation were self
pollinated in the second filial generation(F) generation) 75% pea plants were
found tall and 25% were found dwarf, i.e. the phenotypic ratio of tall and
dwarf pea plant was found 3:1, and the genotypic ratio of the pure tall, hybrid
tall and pure dwarf was 1:2:1. When the obtained pea plants of F2 generation
were self pollinated, in F; generation it was found that pure tall pea plant of F>
generation produced tall plants only. Hybrid pea plants produced both tall and
dwarf in the ratio 3:1, ic. the genotypic ratio of pure tall, hybrid tall and pure
dwarf pea plants was 1:2:1. The dwarf plant produced only dwarf pea plants.
Finally, he concluded with the following results.

i. Pure tall pea plant produced pure tall offsprings only.

ii, Hybrid tall pea plants on self pollination produced both tall and
dwarf offsprings in the phenotypic ratio of 3:1 and genotypic ratio of
pure tall, hybrid tall and pure dwarf 1:2:1.

iii, Pure dwarf pea plant produced pure dwarf offsprings only.

Mendel’s Laws

After many experiments on pea plants, Mendel formulated the following laws.
i. Law of dominance

ii, Law of segregation or purity of gametes

iii, Law of independent assortment

Here, we’ll learn about the first two laws.

1. Law of dominance:

Mendel’s law of dominance states that, “when a cross is made between
two pure individuals having a pair of contrasting characters, only one
character appears externally in the first generation called dominant
character and another remains hidden as the recessive character.” For
example, in a cross between pure tall pea plant (TT) and pure dwarf pea
plant (tt), all the pea plants produced in F, generation appear tall as the

234


dominant character (trait) and dwarf remains hidden as the recessive
character (trait).

Law of segregation or law of purity of gametes

Mendel’s law of segregation or the law of purity of gametes states that
the hybrids of F, generation possess heterozygous alleles for a character,
one is of dominant nature and another is of recessive nature. They remain
together for a long time but separate or segregate at the time of gamete
formation. Due to separation or segregation of alleles or characters a
gamete contains only one of the alleles either of dominant nature or of
recessive nature. It means gametes formed are pure. So, this law is also
called the law of purity of gametes.

For example, in a monohybrid cross between pure tall pea plant and pure
dwarf pea plant, hybrids of F; generation contain both alleles (T) and (t).
During gamete formation they separate or segregate so that each gamete
bears either (T) or (t). As a result, gametes formed become pure.

Mendel’s Law of dominance and Law of purity of gametes are equally
applicable in the case of animals as well. Guinea pig can be used for this
purpose. Their life span is shorter so that many generations of them can
be observed within a short duration.

When a cross is made between pure black Guinea pig (BB) and pure
white Guinea pig (bb), all the Guinea pigs produced in the first
generation will be of black colour. It proves that black colour in the
Guinea pig remains as a dominant character and white colour remains as
a recessive character. When the guinea pigs of F; generation are crossed
together, 75% of guinea pigs produced are seen black and 25% of them
are seen white. If their genetic study is made, 25% Guinea pigs of F>
generation are found pure black, 50% of the Guinea pigs are found
hybrid black and 25% are found pure white, i.c. The phenotypic ratio of
black and white Guinea pigs in F) generation is 3:1 and that of genotypic
ratio of the pure black, hybrid black and pure white Guinea pigs is 1:2:1.

Variation: Although the offsprings resemble their parents in many aspects,
they are not exactly identical. The differences among the individuals may be
in regard to skin colour, height, body structure etc. Hence, the physiological
difference among the individuals of the same species which gives them
individuality is called variation.

Variation is divided into two types.

 

ii,

Hereditary variation or genetic variation
Environmental variation

Hereditary variation: This type of variation is mainly caused due to
structural and functional change in the genes found in the
chromosomes at the time of gamete formation by meiosis cell division. It
is common in all the organisms which reproduce by _ sexual methods.

Environmental variation: Environment plays a great role in bringing
variation among the individuals. The factors of this variation are
light, temperature, air, availability of food etc. Even the offsprings
produced from the same parents become different phenotypically
due to these factors. Hence, the variation that occurs among the
organisms due to environmental factors is called environmental
variation.

On the basis of the degree of difference, variation is classified into two types.

i

 

ii,

Continuous variation
Discontinuous variation

Continuous variation: It is a variation which occurs gradually in the
organisms. For example, difference in height, skin colour, eye colour,
etc.

Discontinuous variation: It is a variation which occurs suddenly in the
organism, caused due to mutation. For examples, six digits in the limbs,
tailless kitten, etc.

Mutation

A sudden and discontinuous variation that occurs in the organisms is called
mutation. The factors for mutation are X-rays, gamma rays and different
chemicals which are called mutagens. For example, a four legged chicken, a
baby produced with six digits, etc.

239

genetic composition of an organism.

characteristic.

pure contrasting characters is called hybrid.
